## IUCN World Conservation Congress and Red List

- Topic: Biodiversity
- Pages: 6-7
- Rationale: Covers the 2025 IUCN Congress resolutions and the latest updates to the Red List, including specific Indian bird species and keystone marine species.

### India's National Red List Roadmap and Vision 2025-2030

- Pages: 6-7
- Focus: India's 5-year initiative to assess species extinction risks and publish National Red Data Books.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Aims to assess extinction risk of ~11,000 species over 5 years.
- Goal to publish National Red Data Books for flora and fauna by 2030.
- Nodal Agency: Union Ministry of Environment, Forest & Climate Change.
- Prepared by ZSI and BSI in collaboration with IUCN India and Centre for Species Survival.
- Internationally aligned with CBD and Kunming-Montreal Global Biodiversity Framework (KMGBF).

#### Section Excerpt

India unveiled its National Red List Roadmap and Vision 2025-2030 to establish a nationally coordinated red listing system. It is a 5-year initiative aiming to assess the extinction risk of approximately 11,000 species and publish National Red Data Books for flora and fauna by 2030. It is prepared by ZSI and BSI in collaboration with IUCN India and aligns with the CBD and KMGBF.

#### Claims

- India's National Red List Roadmap and Vision 2025-2030 is a 5-year initiative aiming to assess the extinction risk of approximately 11,000 species.
- The roadmap aims to publish National Red Data Books for both flora and fauna by the year 2030.
- The Union Ministry of Environment, Forest & Climate Change is the nodal agency for India's National Red List Roadmap.
- The National Red List Roadmap was prepared by the Zoological Survey of India (ZSI) and Botanical Survey of India (BSI) in collaboration with IUCN India and the Centre for Species Survival.
- India's National Red List Roadmap is internationally aligned with the Convention on Biological Diversity (CBD) and the Kunming-Montreal Global Biodiversity Framework (KMGBF).

### IUCN World Conservation Congress and Organizational Overview

- Pages: 6
- Focus: Outcomes of the 2025 Congress in Abu Dhabi and the foundational details of the IUCN.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- Congress 2025 held in Abu Dhabi, UAE.
- Adopted 'Unite for Nature on the Path to 2045' 20-year Strategic Vision.
- First-ever motion advocating for a just phase-out of fossil fuels adopted.
- IUCN founded in 1948, located in Gland, Switzerland.
- Members' Assembly is the highest governing body, held every 4 years.
- Publishes IUCN Report on Protected Areas and IUCN World Heritage Outlook.

#### Section Excerpt

The IUCN World Conservation Congress 2025 took place in Abu Dhabi, adopting a 20-year Strategic Vision 'Unite for Nature on the Path to 2045'. Key resolutions included a just phase-out of fossil fuels, recognizing wild animals as climate allies, and the first World Summit of Indigenous Peoples and Nature. IUCN, founded in 1948 and headquartered in Gland, Switzerland, is governed by the Members' Assembly and IUCN Council.

#### Claims

- The IUCN World Conservation Congress 2025 was held in Abu Dhabi, UAE.
- At the 2025 Congress, the IUCN adopted a 20-year Strategic Vision titled 'Unite for Nature on the Path to 2045'.
- The IUCN World Conservation Congress 2025 adopted the first-ever motion advocating for a just phase-out of fossil fuels.
- The IUCN was founded in 1948 and is headquartered in Gland, Switzerland.
- The Members' Assembly is the highest governing body of the IUCN and convenes every four years.
- The IUCN publishes the IUCN Report on Protected Areas and the IUCN World Heritage Outlook.

### IUCN Key Instruments: Red List and Green Status of Species

- Pages: 7
- Focus: Details of the IUCN Red List categories, recent species status updates, and the IUCN Green Status framework.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- IUCN Red List divides species into 9 categories (NE, DD, LC, NT, VU, EN, CR, EW, EX).
- Indian Courser, Indian Roller, and Rufous-tailed Lark uplisted to Near Threatened.
- Long-billed Grasshopper-warbler uplisted to Endangered.
- Green Sea Turtle improved from Endangered to Least Concern.
- IUCN Green Status of Species launched in 2021 to measure conservation success, complementing the Red List.
- Green Status includes 8 categories and a Green Score (0-100%) indicating proximity to full recovery.

#### Section Excerpt

The IUCN Red List of Threatened Species, established in 1964, divides species into 9 categories from Not Evaluated to Extinct. Recent updates uplisted Indian birds like the Indian Courser and Indian Roller to Near Threatened, and the Long-billed Grasshopper-warbler to Endangered. The IUCN Green Status of Species, launched in 2021, complements the Red List by measuring conservation success and recovery across 8 categories, using a Green Score from 0 to 100%.

#### Claims

- The IUCN Red List of Threatened Species categorizes species into 9 distinct categories ranging from Not Evaluated to Extinct.
- The IUCN Green Status of Species measures conservation success and species recovery using 8 categories and a Green Score ranging from 0 to 100%.
- The Indian Courser and Indian Roller have been recently uplisted to the Near Threatened category on the IUCN Red List.
- The Long-billed Grasshopper-warbler has been uplisted to the Endangered category on the IUCN Red List.
- The conservation status of the Green Sea Turtle has improved from Endangered to Least Concern on the IUCN Red List.

## IUCN Commissions and Green Status

- Topic: Biodiversity
- Pages: 8
- Rationale: Focuses on IUCN technical commissions (WCPA, SSC), the Kenton R. Miller Award, and the Green Status of species like the Lion.

### Green Status Assessment for the Lion

- Pages: 8
- Focus: IUCN Green Status for the Lion, Asiatic Lion conservation profile, and morphological differences between Asiatic and African Lions.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- The First Green Status Assessment for the Lion categorizes its status as 'Largely depleted'.
- The Asiatic Lion is listed as Vulnerable (VU) on the IUCN Red List, Schedule I of WPA 1972, and Appendix I of CITES.
- Asiatic lions have a distinctive longitudinal skin fold along the belly, which is usually absent in African lions.
- Male Asiatic lions have sparser and shorter manes compared to African lions, making their ears almost always visible.
- Asiatic lions are generally smaller in body size than African lions.

#### Section Excerpt

IUCN releases First Green Status Assessment for the Lion (Panthera leo). Lion’s Green Status: ‘Largely depleted’. Extinct Regions: North Africa and Southwest Asia. Asiatic Lion Profile: WPA, 1972: Schedule I; CITES: Appendix I; IUCN Status: VU (Vulnerable). Difference between Asiatic and African Lions: Asiatic Lion has sparser and shorter mane (ears visible), a distinctive longitudinal skin fold along the belly, and is slightly smaller than African lions.

#### Claims

- The IUCN Green Status Assessment categorizes the lion as 'Largely depleted'.
- The Asiatic Lion is classified as Vulnerable on the IUCN Red List, listed in Schedule I of WPA 1972, and Appendix I of CITES.
- Asiatic lions possess a distinctive longitudinal skin fold along their belly, which is typically absent in African lions.
- Male Asiatic lions have sparser and shorter manes compared to African lions, making their ears almost always visible.

### IUCN Species Survival Commission (SSC)

- Pages: 8
- Focus: Establishment, role, and recent Indian leadership appointment to the IUCN SSC.
- Question quota hint: 1
- Claim count: 3

#### Evidence Bullets

- The IUCN Species Survival Commission (SSC) was established in 1949 as a science-based network of volunteer experts.
- Indian wildlife conservationist Vivek Menon was elected as the new Chair of the IUCN SSC for 2025-2029.
- The SSC provides information to IUCN on biodiversity conservation, species value, and ecosystem health.

#### Section Excerpt

Established in 1949, it is a science-based network of more than 10,500 volunteer experts. India’s renowned wildlife conservationist, Vivek Menon, has been elected as the new Chair of the IUCN Species Survival Commission (SSC) for the 2025-2029. Role: To provide information to IUCN on biodiversity conservation, the inherent value of species, their role in ecosystem health and functioning, etc.

#### Claims

- The IUCN Species Survival Commission (SSC) was established in 1949 and operates as a science-based network of volunteer experts.
- Indian wildlife conservationist Vivek Menon was elected as the Chair of the IUCN Species Survival Commission for the 2025-2029 term.
- The role of the IUCN Species Survival Commission is to provide information to the IUCN regarding biodiversity conservation, species value, and ecosystem health.

- Khangchendzonga National Park is the only Indian site that remained 'Good' in the Conservation Outlook Rating.
- Sundarbans National Park's conservation outlook deteriorated from 'Good with some Concerns' to 'Significant Concerns' between 2020 and 2025.

#### Section Excerpt

It categorises Western Ghats and 2 national parks (NP) in India – Assam’s Manas NP and West Bengal’s Sundarbans NP as being of “significant concern”. Other findings: Only Khangchendzonga National Park remained ‘Good’ in Conservation Outlook Rating. Sundarbans National Park: Witnessed Deterioration in its conservation outlook status from ‘Good with some Concerns’ to ‘Significant Concerns’ from 2020-2025.

#### Claims

- The IUCN World Heritage Outlook 4 Report categorizes the Western Ghats, Manas National Park, and Sundarbans National Park as being of 'significant concern'.
- Khangchendzonga National Park is the only Indian site that remained 'Good' in the Conservation Outlook Rating of the IUCN World Heritage Outlook 4 Report.
- The conservation outlook status of Sundarbans National Park deteriorated from 'Good with some Concerns' to 'Significant Concerns' between 2020 and 2025.

### Kenton R. Miller Award

- Pages: 8
- Focus: Details of the Kenton R. Miller Award and the IUCN World Commission on Protected Areas (WCPA).
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 3

#### Evidence Bullets

- Kaziranga Director is the first Indian to receive the Kenton R. Miller Award.
- The award is constituted by the World Commission on Protected Areas (WCPA), one of IUCN's six technical commissions.
- Established in 2006, it honors innovation and leadership in conserving national parks and protected areas.
- The award is presented every two years by the IUCN-WCPA.

#### Section Excerpt

Kaziranga Director becomes first Indian to receive IUCN’s Kenton R. Miller Award for innovation in national parks. Constituted by: World Commission on Protected Areas (WCPA), one of six technical commissions of IUCN. Established in 2006 for Innovation in National Parks and Protected Area Sustainability. Honors individuals who show innovation and leadership in conserving national parks and protected areas. Presented every two years by the IUCN-WCPA.

#### Claims

- The Kenton R. Miller Award is constituted by the World Commission on Protected Areas (WCPA), which is one of the six technical commissions of the IUCN.
- The Kenton R. Miller Award is presented every two years to honor individuals demonstrating innovation and leadership in conserving national parks and protected areas.
- The Director of Kaziranga National Park is the first Indian to receive the IUCN's Kenton R. Miller Award.

- Question quota hint: 1

### Wildlife Protection Act (WPA), 1972 and 2022 Amendments

- Pages: 10
- Focus: The structural changes to WPA schedules, institutional bodies, and rules regarding hunting exceptions and translocation.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- WPA 2022 Amendment reduced schedules to 4: Sch I (highest protection), Sch II (lesser protection), Sch III (plants), Sch IV (CITES species).
- Establishes the National Board for Wildlife, chaired by the Prime Minister.
- Chief Wild Life Warden can permit hunting of Schedule I and II animals under Sections 11 and 12 if they are dangerous to human life or diseased beyond recovery.
- Translocation of Schedule I animals requires prior permission from the Central Government.

#### Section Excerpt

Kerala introduced amendments to ease procedures for killing wild animals threatening humans. The WPA 1972 (2022 Amendment) features 4 Schedules: Schedule I (highest protection), II (lesser protection), III (protected plants), and IV (CITES species). It establishes bodies like the National Board for Wildlife (chaired by PM) and WCCB. Sections 11 and 12 allow the Chief Wild Life Warden to permit hunting of Schedule I and II animals in specific cases (e.g., threat to human life, disease). Translocation requires Central Government permission for Schedule I animals.

#### Claims

- The Wildlife Protection Act (WPA) 2022 Amendment reduced the number of schedules to four, designating Schedule III for protected plants and Schedule IV for CITES species.
- The National Board for Wildlife, established under the Wildlife Protection Act, is chaired by the Prime Minister.
- The Chief Wild Life Warden is authorized to permit the hunting of Schedule I and II animals if they pose a threat to human life or are diseased beyond recovery.
- Prior permission from the Central Government is mandatory for the translocation of Schedule I animals.

### UNESCO's World Network of Biosphere Reserves (WNBR) & Cold Desert BR

- Pages: 9-10
- Focus: The MAB programme, WNBR designation criteria, India's Biosphere Reserves, and specific ecological and cultural details of the Cold Desert BR.
- Question quota hint: 5
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- WNBR (launched 1976) is managed by UNESCO's Man and the Biosphere (MAB) Programme (launched 1971).
- Designations are made by the Director-General of UNESCO following MAB ICC decisions, using the Seville Strategy.
- India has 13 Biosphere Reserves recognized under WNBR.
- Cold Desert BR (Himachal Pradesh) covers Pin Valley NP, Chandratal, and Sarchu & Kibber WLS.
- Cold Desert BR lies in a rain-shadow zone with distinct Buddhist culture (Gompas, Chorten) and fauna like Snow Leopard and Himalayan Ibex.

#### Section Excerpt

UNESCO designated India's Cold Desert Biosphere Reserve to the WNBR. WNBR is managed by the MAB Programme (1971). Designation is by the UNESCO Director-General via the MAB ICC using the Seville Strategy. India has 13 BRs in the WNBR. The Cold Desert BR in Himachal Pradesh covers Pin Valley NP, Chandratal, and Sarchu & Kibber WLS. It features a cold desert biome (rain-shadow zone), distinct Buddhist culture (Gompas, Chorten, Mani Walls), and fauna like the Snow Leopard and Himalayan Ibex.

#### Claims

- The World Network of Biosphere Reserves (WNBR) is managed by UNESCO's Man and the Biosphere (MAB) Programme, which was launched in 1971.
- Designations to the WNBR are made by the Director-General of UNESCO following decisions by the MAB International Coordinating Council (ICC) using the Seville Strategy.
- India has 13 Biosphere Reserves recognized under UNESCO's World Network of Biosphere Reserves (WNBR).
- The Cold Desert Biosphere Reserve in Himachal Pradesh encompasses Pin Valley National Park, Chandratal, and the Sarchu and Kibber Wildlife Sanctuaries.
- The Cold Desert Biosphere Reserve is located in a rain-shadow zone and is characterized by a distinct Buddhist culture and fauna such as the Snow Leopard and Himalayan Ibex.

### Asiatic Lions Conservation and Barda Wildlife Sanctuary

- Pages: 9
- Focus: Conservation initiatives for Asiatic Lions, specifically Project Lion and the emergence of Barda Wildlife Sanctuary as a secondary habitat.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Project Lion (2020) aims to expand habitats beyond Gir to Girnar, Pania, Mitiyala, and Barda.
- Barda Wildlife Sanctuary (Porbandar and Devbhumi Dwarka, Gujarat) is emerging as a second home for Asiatic Lions.
- WPA 2022 amendment places CITES-listed species under Schedule IV of the Act.

#### Section Excerpt

Project Lion was announced in 2020 to expand lion habitats beyond Gir to areas like Girnar, Pania, Mitiyala, and Barda Wildlife Sanctuary (Gujarat). Under the WPA 2022 amendment, CITES-listed species are now under Schedule IV. Barda's key flora includes Gorad, Babul, and Bamboo, and fauna includes Sambar, chital, and chinkara.

#### Claims

- Project Lion was announced in 2020 to expand Asiatic Lion habitats beyond Gir to areas including Barda Wildlife Sanctuary.
- Barda Wildlife Sanctuary is located in Gujarat and is emerging as a second home for Asiatic Lions.
- The Wildlife Protection Act (WPA) 2022 amendment places CITES-listed species under Schedule IV.
- The flora of Barda Wildlife Sanctuary includes Gorad, Babul, and Bamboo, and its fauna includes Sambar, chital, and chinkara.

## Biological Diversity Act and ABS Regulations

- Topic: Biodiversity
- Pages: 11-12
- Rationale: Details the 2025 ABS Regulations, Digital Sequence Information (DSI), and the statutory three-tier implementation system of the BD Act 2002.

### Biological Diversity (Access and Benefit Sharing) Regulation 2025

- Pages: 11
- Focus: Key highlights of the 2025 ABS regulations, including Digital Sequence Information (DSI), Prior Informed Consent (PIC), and benefit-sharing slabs.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Notified by NBA to replace 2014 Guidelines under BDA 2002.
- Includes Digital Sequence Information (DSI) - digital representations of genetic resources like DNA/RNA.
- Exempts access to cultivated medicinal plants notified under BDA 2002.
- Benefit sharing slabs range from nil (up to 5 Cr turnover) to 0.6% (above 250 Cr turnover).
- High-Value Biological Resources (e.g., red sanders) require minimum 5% of auction/sale proceeds.

#### Section Excerpt

Notified by the National Biodiversity Authority (NBA) to replace 2014 Guidelines. Includes Digital Sequence Information (DSI) as a mode of benefit sharing. Requires Prior Informed Consent (PIC) for accessing biological resources. Slabs based on annual turnover range from nil (up to 5 Crore) to 0.6% (above 250 Crore). High-value resources require at least 5% of auction/sale proceeds.

#### Claims

- The Biological Diversity (Access and Benefit Sharing) Regulation 2025 was notified by the National Biodiversity Authority (NBA) to replace the 2014 Guidelines.
- The 2025 ABS Regulations explicitly include Digital Sequence Information (DSI), which are digital representations of genetic resources like DNA/RNA, as a mode of benefit sharing.
- Access to cultivated medicinal plants notified under the Biological Diversity Act 2002 is exempted from the 2025 ABS Regulations.
- Under the 2025 ABS Regulations, entities with an annual turnover of up to 5 Crore are subject to a 'nil' benefit sharing slab.
- High-Value Biological Resources, such as red sanders, require a minimum of 5% of auction or sale proceeds to be shared as benefits under the 2025 ABS Regulations.

### International Framework: CBD, Bonn Guidelines, and Nagoya Protocol

- Pages: 11
- Focus: The Convention on Biological Diversity (CBD) and its associated protocols and guidelines governing Access and Benefit Sharing globally.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- CBD adopted at 1992 Rio Earth Summit; India is a member.
- Three main goals of CBD: Conservation, Sustainable use, Fair and Equitable Benefit-Sharing.
- Cartagena Protocol (2003) deals with Biosafety.
- Nagoya Protocol (2010) is a legally-binding agreement for fair and equitable sharing of benefits from genetic resources.
- Bonn Guidelines (2002) are voluntary guidelines for ABS.
- Multilateral Mechanism for DSI use adopted at CBD COP16 (2024).

#### Section Excerpt

The Convention on Biological Diversity (CBD) is a 1992 Rio Earth Summit treaty with 196 members, including India. Its goals are conservation, sustainable use, and fair benefit-sharing. Major protocols include Cartagena (Biosafety) and Nagoya (ABS). The Bonn Guidelines (2002) are voluntary for ABS, while the Nagoya Protocol (2010) is legally binding.

#### Claims

- The Convention on Biological Diversity (CBD) was adopted at the 1992 Rio Earth Summit and India is a member state.
- The three main goals of the Convention on Biological Diversity are conservation, sustainable use, and fair and equitable benefit-sharing.
- The Cartagena Protocol, adopted under the CBD, deals specifically with Biosafety.
- The Nagoya Protocol is a legally-binding agreement for the fair and equitable sharing of benefits arising from genetic resources.
- The Bonn Guidelines are voluntary guidelines for Access and Benefit-Sharing (ABS).
- A Multilateral Mechanism for the use of Digital Sequence Information (DSI) was adopted at CBD COP16 in 2024.

### CSS-Integrated Development of Wildlife Habitats Scheme (CSS-IDWH)

- Pages: 12
- Focus: Scheme details, nodal ministry, funding structure, and the Species Recovery Programme.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Centrally Sponsored Scheme under the Ministry of Environment, Forest and Climate Change (MoEFCC).
- Funds Protected Areas, wildlife outside PAs, and critically endangered species recovery programmes.
- 22 species currently included, such as Snow Leopard, Asiatic Lion, and Gangetic River Dolphin.
- Gharial and Sloth Bear recommended for inclusion in the Species Recovery Programme.
- Key components include Project Tiger and Project Elephant.

#### Section Excerpt

CSS-IDWH is a Centrally Sponsored Scheme under MoEFCC for wildlife protection. It provides financial assistance to State/UTs for Protected Areas, wildlife outside protected areas, and recovery programmes for critically endangered species. 22 species are included, with Gharial and Sloth Bear recently recommended. Key components include Development of Wildlife Habitats, Project Tiger, and Project Elephant.

#### Claims

- CSS-IDWH is a Centrally Sponsored Scheme administered by the Ministry of Environment, Forest and Climate Change (MoEFCC).
- The CSS-IDWH scheme provides financial assistance for wildlife conservation both inside and outside Protected Areas.
- The Species Recovery Programme under CSS-IDWH currently includes 22 species, such as the Snow Leopard, Asiatic Lion, and Gangetic River Dolphin.
- The Gharial and Sloth Bear have been recommended for inclusion in the Species Recovery Programme under CSS-IDWH.
- Project Tiger and Project Elephant are key components of the CSS-Integrated Development of Wildlife Habitats Scheme (CSS-IDWH).

### India's Legal Framework on ABS (Biological Diversity Act, 2002)

- Pages: 12
- Focus: Statutory framework under the BD Act 2002, its three-tier implementation system, and notable case studies.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- BD Act 2002 covers all biological resources in India, which is broader than the Nagoya Protocol.
- Implemented via a three-tier system: NBA (Central), SBBs (State), and BMCs (Local).
- National Biodiversity Authority (NBA) possesses the powers of a civil court.
- Kani tribal community of Kerala shares benefits from the Jeevani drug developed from the Arogyapacha plant.

#### Section Excerpt

The Biological Diversity Act, 2002 covers all biological resources within India, broader than the Nagoya Protocol's scope. It features a three-tier statutory system: National Biodiversity Authority (NBA) with civil court powers, State Biodiversity Boards (SBBs), and Biodiversity Management Committees (BMCs) at the local level. A key case study is the Kani tribal community's benefit sharing for the Jeevani drug from the Arogyapacha plant.

#### Claims

- The Biological Diversity Act, 2002 covers all biological resources within India, making its scope broader than that of the Nagoya Protocol.
- The Biological Diversity Act, 2002 is implemented through a three-tier statutory system consisting of the National Biodiversity Authority (NBA), State Biodiversity Boards (SBBs), and local Biodiversity Management Committees (BMCs).
- The National Biodiversity Authority (NBA) possesses the powers of a civil court.
- The Kani tribal community of Kerala participates in benefit sharing for the Jeevani drug, which is developed from the Arogyapacha plant.

### Species Profile: Sloth Bear

- Pages: 12
- Focus: Characteristics, habitat, threats, and conservation status of the Sloth Bear.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Specializes in eating termites and ants and does not hibernate unlike other bears.
- Native to forests and grasslands of India, Nepal, and Sri Lanka.
- Conservation Status: WPA 1972 Schedule I, CITES Appendix I, IUCN Vulnerable (VU).
- Daroji Sloth Bear Sanctuary in Karnataka is the first dedicated sloth bear sanctuary in Asia.
- Project Sloth Bear initiated in 2025.

#### Section Excerpt

The Sloth Bear specializes in eating termites and ants and does not hibernate. It is solitary, nocturnal, and native to India, Nepal, and Sri Lanka. Its conservation status is Schedule I under WPA 1972, Appendix I under CITES, and Vulnerable (VU) under IUCN. Daroji Sloth Bear Sanctuary in Karnataka is Asia's first dedicated sloth bear sanctuary.

#### Claims

- The Sloth Bear specializes in eating termites and ants and, unlike other bears, does not hibernate.
- The Sloth Bear is native to the forests and grasslands of India, Nepal, and Sri Lanka.
- The Sloth Bear is classified as Vulnerable (VU) on the IUCN Red List, listed under Schedule I of the Wildlife Protection Act (WPA) 1972, and included in CITES Appendix I.
- Daroji Sloth Bear Sanctuary, located in Karnataka, is the first dedicated sloth bear sanctuary in Asia.
- Project Sloth Bear was initiated in the year 2025 to focus on the conservation of the species.

## Project Cheetah and Tiger Conservation

- Topic: Biodiversity
- Pages: 13-14
- Rationale: Combines the reintroduction of Cheetahs with Supreme Court directions on Tiger Reserve management and Human-Wildlife Conflict mitigation.

### Project Cheetah and Associated Habitats

- Pages: 13-14
- Focus: Cheetah reintroduction details, comparison of Asiatic and African cheetahs, and Gandhi Sagar Wildlife Sanctuary profile.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Project Cheetah operates under Project Tiger and Elephant; implemented by NTCA with WII technical assistance.
- Asiatic Cheetah: IUCN Critically Endangered, CITES Appendix I, WPA Schedule I; found only in Iran; extinct in India since 1952.
- African Cheetah: IUCN Vulnerable, CITES Appendix I; Namibia holds the largest population.
- Cheetahs are the world's fastest mammals and do not roar unlike other big cats.
- Gandhi Sagar WLS: Located in MP (Nimar region), features Khathiar-Gir dry deciduous forest, River Chambal, and Chaturbhuj Nala rock shelters.

#### Section Excerpt

Project Cheetah, launched in 2022 under the umbrella of Project Tiger and Elephant, aims to reintroduce cheetahs to India. Implemented by NTCA with WII's technical assistance, it currently supports cheetahs in Kuno National Park. The Asiatic Cheetah is Critically Endangered (IUCN) and found only in Iran, while the African Cheetah is Vulnerable. Gandhi Sagar Wildlife Sanctuary in Madhya Pradesh (Khathiar-Gir dry deciduous forest, River Chambal) is also highlighted, featuring flora like Salai and fauna like Dholes and Mugger crocodiles, alongside Chaturbhuj Nala rock shelters.

#### Claims

- Project Cheetah is implemented by the National Tiger Conservation Authority (NTCA) with technical assistance from the Wildlife Institute of India (WII).
- The Asiatic Cheetah is classified as Critically Endangered by the IUCN, whereas the African Cheetah is classified as Vulnerable.
- The Asiatic Cheetah is currently found only in Iran and has been extinct in India since 1952.
- Cheetahs are the world's fastest mammals and, unlike other big cats, they do not roar.
- Gandhi Sagar Wildlife Sanctuary in Madhya Pradesh features the River Chambal, Khathiar-Gir dry deciduous forests, and Chaturbhuj Nala rock shelters.

### Tiger Reserves and Supreme Court Directives

- Pages: 14
- Focus: Supreme Court guidelines on tiger reserve management, core vs. buffer zone regulations, and statutory provisions for Tiger Reserves under WPA 1972.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- SC banned tiger safaris and night tourism in core areas/designated tiger corridors.
- Safaris are permitted only on non-forest or degraded forest land in buffer zones.
- Human-Wildlife Conflicts (HWCs) to be granted Natural Disaster status with 10 lakh ex-gratia for human deaths.
- Tiger Reserves consist of inviolable Core Areas and regulated Buffer Areas (sustainable use zones).
- Reserves are notified by State Governments under WPA 1972 on the advice of NTCA.
- Boundary alterations of Tiger Reserves require NTCA recommendation and NBWL approval.

#### Section Excerpt

The Supreme Court issued directions to protect tiger reserves, banning tiger safaris and night tourism in core/critical habitats. Safaris are permitted only on non-forest or degraded forest land in buffer zones. Commercial mining and major hydro projects are prohibited in buffer areas. Human-Wildlife Conflicts (HWCs) are to be treated as Natural Disasters with a uniform 10 lakh ex-gratia for human deaths. Tiger Reserves are declared under WPA 1972 by State Governments on NTCA's advice, and boundary alterations require NTCA recommendation and National Board for Wild Life (NBWL) approval.

#### Claims

- The Supreme Court has banned tiger safaris and night tourism in the core areas and designated tiger corridors of tiger reserves.
- Tiger safaris are permitted only on non-forest or degraded forest land within the buffer zones of tiger reserves.
- Commercial mining and major hydroelectric projects are prohibited in the buffer areas of tiger reserves.
- Human-Wildlife Conflicts (HWCs) are to be treated as Natural Disasters, providing a uniform 10 lakh ex-gratia for human deaths.
- Tiger Reserves are notified by State Governments under the Wildlife Protection Act (WPA) 1972 on the advice of the National Tiger Conservation Authority (NTCA).
- Altering the boundaries of a Tiger Reserve requires the recommendation of the NTCA and the approval of the National Board for Wild Life (NBWL).

## International Big Cat Alliance and Small Cats

- Topic: Biodiversity
- Pages: 15-16
- Rationale: Covers the treaty-based IBCA and the specific ecological status of Indian small cats like the Caracal and Fishing Cat.

### Status of Small Cats in India

- Pages: 15-16
- Focus: Findings of the WII-NTCA report, characteristics of small cats, and specific profiles of major small cats in India.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Report released by Wildlife Institute of India (WII) and National Tiger Conservation Authority (NTCA).
- India hosts 10 small cat species; Jungle Cat is most widespread, Rusty-Spotted Cat is the world's smallest.
- Small cats have a rigid hyoid bone (purr continuously), whereas big cats have a flexible hyoid bone (roar).
- Caracal (Siya Gosh) is found only in Rajasthan, Gujarat, and Madhya Pradesh (IUCN: Least Concern).
- Fishing cat (IUCN: Vulnerable) has webbed hind feet and is found in wetland environments like Sundarbans.

#### Section Excerpt

A report by WII and NTCA assessed small cats in India. India has 10 small cat species, with the Jungle Cat being the most widespread and the Rusty-Spotted Cat being the world's smallest. Unlike big cats that have a flexible hyoid bone to roar, small cats have a rigid hyoid bone allowing them to purr. Key species include the Asiatic golden cat (NT, endemic to Indo-Malayan eco-realm), Caracal (LC, arid regions of RJ, GJ, MP), Fishing cat (VU, wetlands), and Eurasian lynx (LC, Ladakh). All are WPA Schedule I.

#### Claims

- India hosts 10 small cat species, with the Jungle Cat being the most widespread and the Rusty-Spotted Cat being the world's smallest.
- Small cats possess a rigid hyoid bone that allows them to purr continuously, unlike big cats which have a flexible hyoid bone enabling them to roar.
- The Caracal is found only in the arid regions of Rajasthan, Gujarat, and Madhya Pradesh and is listed as Least Concern by the IUCN.
- The Fishing cat is a wetland-dependent species with webbed hind feet, found in areas like the Sundarbans, and is listed as Vulnerable by the IUCN.
- All small cat species in India are protected under Schedule I of the Wildlife Protection Act (WPA).

### International Big Cat Alliance (IBCA)

- Pages: 15
- Focus: Genesis, structure, membership, and conservation targets of the IBCA.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- Launched by India in 2023 to mark 50 years of Project Tiger.
- Aims to conserve 7 big cats: Tiger, Lion, Leopard, Snow Leopard, Cheetah, Jaguar, and Puma.
- Puma and Jaguar are not found in India; the rest are protected under WPA 1972 Schedule I.
- Treaty-based inter-governmental organization with 95 member countries and 16 founding members.
- First IBCA assembly hosted in New Delhi in 2025.

#### Section Excerpt

Launched by India in 2023 to commemorate 50 years of Project Tiger, the IBCA is a treaty-based inter-governmental organization. It aims to conserve 7 big cats: Tiger, Lion, Leopard, Snow Leopard, Cheetah, Jaguar, and Puma. All except Puma and Jaguar are found in India and protected under Schedule I of WPA, 1972. It has 95 member countries, with 16 founding members, and its apex decision-making body is the IBCA Assembly, which meets every 2 years.

#### Claims

- The International Big Cat Alliance (IBCA) was launched by India in 2023 to commemorate 50 years of Project Tiger.
- The IBCA is a treaty-based inter-governmental organization.
- The IBCA aims to conserve seven big cats: Tiger, Lion, Leopard, Snow Leopard, Cheetah, Jaguar, and Puma.
- Among the seven big cats conserved by the IBCA, Puma and Jaguar are the only ones not found in India.
- The five IBCA big cats found in India are protected under Schedule I of the Wildlife Protection Act (WPA), 1972.
- The apex decision-making body of the IBCA is the IBCA Assembly, which meets every two years.

### Status of Elephants in India

- Pages: 16
- Focus: Findings of the SAIEE 2021-25 DNA-based elephant count and population distribution.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- SAIEE 2021-25 is India's first DNA-based elephant count, conducted by WII.
- India holds approximately 60% of the global wild Asian Elephant population.
- Western Ghats region hosts the largest population of wild elephants in India.
- Karnataka has the highest state-wise elephant population, followed by Assam, Tamil Nadu, and Kerala.
- A small feral population of elephants exists in the Andaman Islands.

#### Section Excerpt

The Synchronous All India Population Estimation of Elephants (SAIEE) 2021-25 is India's first DNA-based count conducted by WII under Project Elephant. India harbors about 60% of the global Asian Elephant wild population (>22,000). The Western Ghats hosts the largest population, followed by North Eastern Hills. State-wise, Karnataka supports the highest population, followed by Assam, Tamil Nadu, and Kerala.

#### Claims

- The Synchronous All India Population Estimation of Elephants (SAIEE) 2021-25 is India's first DNA-based elephant count, conducted by the Wildlife Institute of India (WII).
- India harbors approximately 60% of the global wild Asian Elephant population.
- The Western Ghats region hosts the largest population of wild elephants in India, followed by the North Eastern Hills.
- Karnataka has the highest state-wise elephant population in India, followed by Assam, Tamil Nadu, and Kerala.
- A small feral population of elephants exists in the Andaman Islands.

## Elephant and Crocodile Conservation

- Topic: Biodiversity
- Pages: 17-18
- Rationale: Compares Asian and African elephants and details the conservation status and habitats of India's three crocodile species.

### Crocodile Conservation Project and Native Species of India

- Pages: 17-18
- Focus: 50 years of the Crocodile Conservation Project, recovery statistics, and the characteristics, habitats, and conservation statuses of Saltwater, Mugger, and Gharial crocodiles.
- Question quota hint: 5
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- The Crocodile Conservation Project was launched in April 1975 based on H.R. Bustard's recommendations.
- Odisha is the only Indian state with conservation centres for all 3 native species: Tikarpada for gharials, Dangamal for saltwater crocodiles, and Ramatirtha for muggers.
- Saltwater Crocodile (Estuarine): IUCN Least Concern; found in brackish water and mangroves like Bhitarkanika, Andaman & Nicobar, and Sundarbans.
- Mugger (Marsh Crocodile): IUCN Vulnerable; known to use tools like balance sticks to lure birds and is the only Indian species that digs burrows on land.
- Gharial: IUCN Critically Endangered; adult males have a bulb-like 'Ghara' on their snout; found in deep, fast-flowing rivers like Chambal, Girwa, and Son.
- India holds 80% of the global wild gharial population, supported by sanctuaries like the National Chambal Sanctuary.

#### Section Excerpt

India celebrated 50 years of its Crocodile Conservation Project, launched in 1975. Odisha is the only state with conservation centres for all three native species: Saltwater (Dangamal), Mugger (Ramatirtha), and Gharial (Tikarpada). India holds 80% of the global wild gharial population. The three native species are the Saltwater Crocodile (Least Concern), Mugger (Vulnerable), and Gharial (Critically Endangered). Gharials are expert fish catchers with a unique 'Ghara' on males' snouts, while Muggers use tools and dig burrows.

#### Claims

- The Crocodile Conservation Project was launched in India in 1975 based on the recommendations of H.R. Bustard.
- Odisha is the only Indian state that has conservation centres for all three native crocodile species: Saltwater, Mugger, and Gharial.
- The Saltwater Crocodile is classified as Least Concern on the IUCN Red List and inhabits brackish water and mangroves.
- The Mugger crocodile is classified as Vulnerable and is the only Indian crocodile species that digs burrows on land and uses tools to lure prey.
- The Gharial is a Critically Endangered species found in deep, fast-flowing rivers, and adult males possess a unique bulb-like 'Ghara' on their snout.
- India is home to 80% of the global wild gharial population, with major populations in the National Chambal Sanctuary.

### Asian Elephants and Comparison with African Elephants

- Pages: 17
- Focus: Characteristics, habitat, conservation status of Asian Elephants, and morphological differences from African Elephants.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Asian Elephants have a matriarchal social structure and the longest gestation period in mammals at 22 months.
- Conservation Status of Asian Elephants: IUCN Endangered, WPA 1972 Schedule I, and CITES Appendix I.
- Project Elephant was launched in 1992, and there are currently 33 Elephant Reserves across 14 major states.
- Asian elephants have a twin-domed head with a central indent and a humped/convex back, whereas African elephants have a rounded/flat single dome and a concave/dipped back.
- Only some male Asian elephants have visible tusks, while both male and female African elephants have larger tusks.

#### Section Excerpt

Asian Elephants (Elephas maximus) are the largest land mammals in Asia, featuring a matriarchal social structure and a 22-month gestation period. They are classified as Endangered by IUCN, Schedule I under WPA, and Appendix I under CITES. Project Elephant was launched in 1992. Key differences from African elephants include smaller size, twin-domed head, smaller rounded ears, humped back, and tusks typically only visible in some males.

#### Claims

- Asian Elephants have a matriarchal social structure and a 22-month gestation period, which is the longest among mammals.
- The conservation status of Asian Elephants is Endangered under the IUCN Red List, Schedule I under the WPA 1972, and Appendix I under CITES.
- Project Elephant was launched in 1992 and currently comprises 33 Elephant Reserves across 14 major states.
- Asian elephants possess a twin-domed head and a humped or convex back, whereas African elephants have a single-domed head and a concave or dipped back.
- Only some male Asian elephants have visible tusks, whereas both male and female African elephants have larger tusks.

## River Dolphins and Vultures

- Topic: Biodiversity
- Pages: 19-20
- Rationale: Focuses on the 2024 population estimates for Gangetic and Indus dolphins and the pan-India assessment of critically endangered vultures.

### River Dolphins in India

- Pages: 19
- Focus: Population status, species differentiation (Gangetic vs. Indus), biological characteristics, and conservation initiatives.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- India's river dolphin population is 6327, comprising 6324 Gangetic and only 3 Indus River Dolphins.
- Both Gangetic and Indus River Dolphins are listed as Endangered (EN) on the IUCN Red List and Schedule I of WPA, 1972.
- River dolphins are functionally blind and rely on echolocation to navigate and hunt.
- The Gangetic Dolphin is the National Aquatic Animal of India, while the Indus River Dolphin is Punjab's State Aquatic Animal.
- Key conservation initiatives include Project Dolphin, Vikramshila Gangetic Dolphin Wildlife Sanctuary in Bihar, and India's first National Dolphin Research Centre (NDRC) at Patna.

#### Section Excerpt

Ministry of Environment, Forest and Climate Change and Wildlife Institute of India (WII) released 'Population Status of River Dolphins in India 2024'... India’s River dolphin population: 6327- Gangetic River dolphins (6324), Indus River Dolphins (only 3)... Ganges and Indus River Dolphins are two distinct species based on genetic and morphological data. Functionally blind, and rely on echolocation to navigate and hunt... Gangetic Dolphin Specifics: National Aquatic Animal of India... Indus River Dolphin Specifics: Punjab’s State Aquatic Animal... Conservation Measures: Project Dolphin (2020), Vikramshila Gangetic Dolphin Wildlife Sanctuary, Bihar, India’s first National Dolphin Research Centre (NDRC) at Patna.

#### Claims

- India hosts two distinct species of river dolphins, the Gangetic and the Indus River Dolphin, with the latter having a population of only 3 individuals in the country.
- Both Gangetic and Indus River Dolphins are functionally blind and use echolocation for navigation and hunting.
- The Gangetic and Indus River Dolphins are both listed as Endangered on the IUCN Red List and protected under Schedule I of the Wildlife Protection Act, 1972.
- The Gangetic Dolphin is recognized as the National Aquatic Animal of India, while the Indus River Dolphin is the State Aquatic Animal of Punjab.
- India's first National Dolphin Research Centre (NDRC) is located in Patna, and Bihar also hosts the Vikramshila Gangetic Dolphin Wildlife Sanctuary.

### Draft Policy Paper on Camels and Camel Conservation

- Pages: 20
- Focus: Population decline drivers, habitat, pastoralist communities, and biological adaptations of camels.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- India's camel population has declined by over 75% since the 1970s.
- 90% of camels in India are reared in the dryland ecosystems of Rajasthan and Gujarat.
- Key camel-rearing pastoralist communities include the Raika, Rabari, Fakirani Jat, and Manganiyar.
- Camels store fat in their humps for energy, while water is stored in their blood cells, not their humps.
- The National Camel Sustainability Initiative (NCSI) was proposed by the Ministry of Fisheries, Animal Husbandry and Dairying and the FAO.

#### Section Excerpt

Draft policy paper on Camels, prepared by Ministry of Fisheries, Animal Husbandry and Dairying and Food and Agriculture Organization (FAO) has proposed National Camel Sustainability Initiative (NCSI)... India’s camel population declined by more than 75% since the 1970s... Habitat: Dryland ecosystems (referred as “Ship of the Desert”) and are primarily reared (90%) in Rajasthan and Gujarat. Camel-rearing Pastoralist communities: Raika, Rabari, Fakirani Jat, and Manganiyar... Camels' humps store fats providing them energy when food is scarce and they store water in their blood cells, not their humps.

#### Claims

- India's camel population has declined by more than 75% since the 1970s.
- Approximately 90% of camels in India are reared in the dryland ecosystems of Rajasthan and Gujarat.
- The Raika, Rabari, Fakirani Jat, and Manganiyar are traditional camel-rearing pastoralist communities in India.
- Camels store fat, not water, in their humps to provide energy during periods of food scarcity.
- Camels store water in their blood cells rather than in their humps.
- The National Camel Sustainability Initiative (NCSI) was proposed by the Ministry of Fisheries, Animal Husbandry and Dairying in collaboration with the Food and Agriculture Organization (FAO).

### Vultures in India and Conservation Efforts

- Pages: 20
- Focus: WII assessment findings, IUCN statuses of the 9 vulture species, threats from NSAIDs, and conservation initiatives.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Four vulture species in India are Critically Endangered (CR): White-rumped, Indian, Slender-billed, and Red-headed vultures.
- The Egyptian Vulture is Endangered (EN), while Bearded, Cinereous, and Himalayan Griffon are Near Threatened (NT).
- A major threat to vultures is the ingestion of toxic non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs) like diclofenac used to treat livestock.
- Vulture Conservation Breeding Centres (VCBC) are located at Rani (Assam) and Pinjore (Haryana).
- Protected Areas hold 54% of all documented vulture nests according to the WII assessment.

#### Section Excerpt

Wildlife Institute of India (WII) releases its 1st Pan-India Assessment and Monitoring of Endangered Species (Vultures)... Focus: Four Critically Endangered species i.e., the White-rumped, Indian, Slender-billed, and Red-headed vultures at 216 sites across 17 states... Threats: Ingestion of toxic non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs) like diclofenac (used to treat livestock)... Conservation Initiatives: Vulture Conservation Breeding Centre (VCBC)- at Rani (Assam), Pinjore (Haryana)... White-rumped Vulture (Gyps bengalensis): Critically Endangered (CR)... Egyptian Vulture (Neophron percnopterus): Endangered (EN).

#### Claims

- Four vulture species in India, namely the White-rumped, Indian, Slender-billed, and Red-headed vultures, are classified as Critically Endangered (CR).
- The Egyptian Vulture is classified as Endangered (EN), while the Bearded, Cinereous, and Himalayan Griffon vultures are Near Threatened (NT).
- The ingestion of toxic non-steroidal anti-inflammatory drugs (NSAIDs) like diclofenac, which are used to treat livestock, is a major threat to vulture populations.
- Vulture Conservation Breeding Centres (VCBC) are located at Rani in Assam and Pinjore in Haryana.
- According to the Wildlife Institute of India (WII) assessment, 54% of all documented vulture nests are located within Protected Areas.

- Bactrian camels have two humps acting as energy (fat) reservoirs, not water.

### Wild and Domestic Yak

- Pages: 21
- Focus: Habitat, characteristics, and conservation status of Wild Yak (Bos mutus) and Domestic Yak (Bos grunniens).
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Wild Yak (Bos mutus) is native to China and India but is regionally extinct from Bhutan.
- Wild Yak conservation status: IUCN Vulnerable (VU), WPA Schedule I, CMS Appendix I.
- Domestic Yak (Bos grunniens) has larger lungs and heart than cattle to adapt to high altitudes.

#### Section Excerpt

Discusses the first-ever chromosome-level genome assembly of the Indian/Domestic Yak. Details the Wild Yak (Bos mutus), native to the Tibetan plateau, China, and India (regionally extinct in Bhutan), listed as Vulnerable (IUCN) and Schedule I (WPA). Contrasts with the Domestic Yak (Bos grunniens), known as the 'ship of the Himalayas'.

#### Claims

- The Wild Yak (Bos mutus) is native to China and India but is considered regionally extinct in Bhutan.
- The Wild Yak is classified as Vulnerable on the IUCN Red List, protected under Schedule I of the Wildlife Protection Act (WPA), and listed in CMS Appendix I.
- The Domestic Yak (Bos grunniens), known as the 'ship of the Himalayas', adapts to high altitudes by possessing larger lungs and a larger heart compared to cattle.
- The first-ever chromosome-level genome assembly has been successfully conducted for the Indian/Domestic Yak.

### Tamil Nadu's Endangered Species Conservation

- Pages: 22
- Focus: Conservation status and key traits of four specific species targeted by Tamil Nadu's conservation fund.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Lion-tailed macaque is Endangered (IUCN), Schedule I (WPA), and endemic to the Western Ghats.
- Madras Hedgehog is Least Concern (IUCN), endemic to India, and is a nocturnal species known locally as the 'thorny rat'.
- Striped Hyaena is Near Threatened (IUCN) and acts as a natural scavenger regulating diseases.
- Hump-headed mahseer fish is Critically Endangered (IUCN) and is known as the 'Tiger of Kaveri'.

#### Section Excerpt

Tamil Nadu sanctioned Rs 1 crore for conserving four species: Lion-tailed macaque (Endangered, endemic to Western Ghats), Madras Hedgehog (Least Concern, endemic to India, known as 'thorny rat'), Striped Hyaena (Near Threatened, scavenger declining in Mudumalai), and Hump-headed mahseer fish (Critically Endangered, endemic to India, called 'Tiger of Kaveri').

#### Claims

- The Lion-tailed macaque is an Endangered species endemic to the Western Ghats and is protected under Schedule I of the Wildlife Protection Act.
- The Madras Hedgehog, locally known as the 'thorny rat', is a nocturnal species endemic to India and is listed as Least Concern by the IUCN.
- The Striped Hyaena is a Near Threatened species that acts as a natural scavenger to regulate diseases, with declining populations noted in Mudumalai.
- The Hump-headed mahseer fish is a Critically Endangered species endemic to India and is commonly referred to as the 'Tiger of Kaveri'.

### Van (Sanrakshan Evam Samvardhan) Amendment Rules, 2025

- Pages: 22
- Focus: Key provisions and changes introduced in the 2025 amendment to the forest conservation rules.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 3

#### Evidence Bullets

- Compensatory Afforestation (CA) land can now be transferred to the Forest Department or notified as a protected area under the Indian Forest Act, 1927.
- Mining of critical/strategic minerals requires CA on degraded forest land at a minimum of double the extent.
- State governments are allowed to grant initial 'working permission' for linear projects like roadworks, railway tracks, and transmission lines.

#### Section Excerpt

Highlights the MoEFCC's Van (Sanrakshan Evam Samvardhan) Amendment Rules, 2025. Key changes include allowing Compensatory Afforestation (CA) land to be transferred to the Forest Department or notified as a protected area. It mandates double the extent of CA on degraded forest land for mining critical/strategic minerals and allows state governments to grant initial 'working permission' for linear projects.

#### Claims

- Under the Van (Sanrakshan Evam Samvardhan) Amendment Rules, 2025, Compensatory Afforestation (CA) land can be transferred to the Forest Department or notified as a protected area under the Indian Forest Act, 1927.
- The Van (Sanrakshan Evam Samvardhan) Amendment Rules, 2025 mandate that mining of critical or strategic minerals requires Compensatory Afforestation on degraded forest land at a minimum of double the extent.
- State governments have the authority to grant initial 'working permission' for linear projects such as roadworks, railway tracks, and transmission lines under the Van (Sanrakshan Evam Samvardhan) Amendment Rules, 2025.

## Forest Conservation Act and Green India Mission

- Topic: Forests
- Pages: 23-24
- Rationale: Covers the 2023 Forest Amendment Act, clearance procedures, and the strategies of the Green India Mission under NAPCC.

### National Mission for a Green India (GIM)

- Pages: 23-24
- Focus: Objectives, funding, implementation mechanisms, and key strategies of the Green India Mission under NAPCC.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- GIM is one of the 8 missions under the National Action Plan on Climate Change (NAPCC).
- Aims to create an additional carbon sink of 2.5 to 3.0 billion tonnes of CO2 equivalent by 2030.
- Funding is partially sourced from the National CAMPA Fund.
- Implementation relies on a bottom-up model with Joint Forest Management Committees (JFMCs).
- Strategies include selling carbon credits from forestry and agroforestry via voluntary markets.

#### Section Excerpt

Launched in 2014 under NAPCC. Objectives include increasing forest/tree cover over 24 million hectares and creating an additional carbon sink of 2.5 to 3.0 billion tonnes of CO2 equivalent by 2030. Funding comes from Mission allocation and National CAMPA Fund. Implemented via a bottom-up model with Joint Forest Management Committees (JFMCs). Strategies include Micro-Ecosystem Approach for vulnerable zones, leveraging CSR, selling carbon credits via voluntary markets, and deploying a Green India Force of trained youth.

#### Claims

- The National Mission for a Green India (GIM) is one of the eight missions under the National Action Plan on Climate Change (NAPCC).
- GIM aims to create an additional carbon sink of 2.5 to 3.0 billion tonnes of CO2 equivalent by 2030.
- Funding for the Green India Mission is partially sourced from the National CAMPA Fund.
- The implementation of GIM relies on a bottom-up model involving Joint Forest Management Committees (JFMCs).
- GIM strategies include selling carbon credits generated from forestry and agroforestry via voluntary markets.

### Forest Clearance and Forest (Conservation) Amendment Act, 2023

- Pages: 23
- Focus: Statutory procedures for forest clearance, redefined scope of 'forest', and exemptions under the 2023 Amendment Act, including the Godavarman judgment context.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Stage I clearance requires conditions like funding compensatory afforestation; Stage II is final clearance.
- MoEFCC grants clearance for projects above 5 hectares or any mining proposals.
- 2023 Act exempts forest land within 100 kms of international borders or LAC for national security projects.
- Exempts up to 5 hectares for defence or public utility projects in Left-Wing Extremism-affected areas.
- Godavarman case (1996) expanded forest definition to include dictionary meaning and all government records irrespective of ownership.

#### Section Excerpt

Procedure for Forest Clearance involves Stage I (In-Principle) and Stage II (Final). Approval Authority varies: up to 5 ha by Chief Conservator, above 5 ha or mining by MoEFCC. The Forest (Conservation) Amendment Act, 2023 defines forest as land notified under laws like Indian Forest Act 1927 or recorded in govt records on/after Oct 25, 1980. It exempts land changed to non-forest use before 1996. Supreme Court directed following the 1996 Godavarman dictionary meaning until identification is complete. Exemptions include land within 100 km of borders for national security, up to 10 ha for security infrastructure, and up to 5 ha in LWE areas.

#### Claims

- Under the forest clearance procedure, the Chief Conservator approves projects up to 5 hectares, whereas the MoEFCC grants clearance for projects above 5 hectares or any mining proposals.
- The Forest (Conservation) Amendment Act, 2023 exempts forest land situated within 100 km of international borders or the Line of Actual Control (LAC) for national security projects.
- The Forest (Conservation) Amendment Act, 2023 provides an exemption of up to 5 hectares of forest land for defence or public utility projects in Left-Wing Extremism (LWE) affected areas.
- The 1996 Godavarman judgment by the Supreme Court expanded the definition of 'forest' to encompass its dictionary meaning and all government records, regardless of ownership.
- The Forest (Conservation) Amendment Act, 2023 defines a forest as land notified under laws like the Indian Forest Act 1927 or recorded in government records on or after October 25, 1980.

### Environmental Accounting on Forest 2025 and ISFR 2023

- Pages: 24
- Focus: MoSPI's forest accounting report based on the UN SEEA framework and the latest forest cover statistics from ISFR 2023.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Environmental Accounting on Forest 2025 Report was released by the Ministry of Statistics and Programme Implementation (MoSPI).
- The report is based on the UN System of Environmental Economic Accounts (SEEA) Framework developed in 2012.
- ISFR 2023 reports total forest and tree cover at 25.17 percent of India's geographical area.
- Maximum increase in forest cover was recorded in Chhattisgarh, Uttar Pradesh, Odisha, and Rajasthan.
- 8 states/UTs, including Mizoram and Lakshadweep, have forest cover above 75 percent of their area.

#### Section Excerpt

Environmental Accounting on Forest 2025 Report released by MoSPI is the first dedicated publication on forest accounting based on the UN System of Environmental Economic Accounts (SEEA) Framework. SEEA integrates economic and environmental data. ISFR 2023 shows total forest and tree cover increased to 25.17 percent of geographical area (21.76 percent Forest cover, 3.41 percent Tree cover). Maximum increase seen in Chhattisgarh, UP, Odisha, and Rajasthan. 8 states/UTs have forest cover above 75 percent, including Mizoram, Lakshadweep, A&N Islands, and Arunachal Pradesh.

#### Claims

- The Environmental Accounting on Forest 2025 Report was released by the Ministry of Statistics and Programme Implementation (MoSPI).
- The Environmental Accounting on Forest 2025 Report is based on the UN System of Environmental Economic Accounts (SEEA) Framework, which integrates economic and environmental data.
- According to ISFR 2023, India's total forest and tree cover stands at 25.17 percent of its geographical area.
- Chhattisgarh, Uttar Pradesh, Odisha, and Rajasthan recorded the maximum increase in forest cover according to ISFR 2023.
- Eight states and Union Territories in India, including Mizoram, Lakshadweep, A&N Islands, and Arunachal Pradesh, have a forest cover of more than 75 percent of their geographical area.

## ISFR 2023 and UN High Seas Treaty

- Topic: Forests and Oceans
- Pages: 25-26
- Rationale: Combines forest/mangrove data from ISFR 2023 with the legal framework of UNCLOS and the BBNJ (High Seas) Treaty.

### UN High Seas Treaty (BBNJ) and UNCLOS

- Pages: 25-26
- Focus: Provisions of the BBNJ Agreement, its relationship with UNCLOS, and the legal boundaries of maritime zones.
- Question quota hint: 5
- Claim count: 7

#### Evidence Bullets

- BBNJ is the third implementation agreement under UNCLOS (others: 1994 Part XI and 1995 Fish Stocks).
- BBNJ requires 60 ratifications to enter into force; India has signed but not ratified.
- Non-UNCLOS members can also join the BBNJ agreement.
- UNCLOS Maritime Zones: Territorial Sea (up to 12 nm, full sovereignty), Contiguous Zone (up to 24 nm), EEZ (up to 200 nm).
- Deep Seabed beyond national jurisdiction is the 'common heritage of mankind', managed by the International Seabed Authority (ISA).

#### Section Excerpt

Covers the BBNJ Agreement (High Seas Treaty), a legally binding treaty under UNCLOS aimed at conserving marine biodiversity beyond national jurisdiction. It outlines key pillars like Marine Protected Areas (MPAs), Marine Genetic Resources (MGRs), and Environmental Impact Assessments. Also details UNCLOS maritime zones: Territorial Sea (12 nm), Contiguous Zone (24 nm), and EEZ (200 nm), along with dispute settlement mechanisms like ITLOS.

#### Claims

- The BBNJ Agreement (High Seas Treaty) is a legally binding treaty under UNCLOS aimed at conserving marine biodiversity beyond national jurisdiction.
- The key pillars of the BBNJ Agreement include the establishment of Marine Protected Areas (MPAs), management of Marine Genetic Resources (MGRs), and conducting Environmental Impact Assessments.
- The BBNJ Agreement is the third implementation agreement under UNCLOS, following the 1994 Part XI and 1995 Fish Stocks agreements.
- The BBNJ Agreement requires 60 ratifications to enter into force, and India has signed but not yet ratified it.
- Non-UNCLOS members are permitted to join the BBNJ agreement.
- Under UNCLOS, the Territorial Sea extends up to 12 nautical miles with full sovereignty, the Contiguous Zone up to 24 nautical miles, and the Exclusive Economic Zone (EEZ) up to 200 nautical miles.
- The Deep Seabed beyond national jurisdiction is designated as the 'common heritage of mankind' and is managed by the International Seabed Authority (ISA).

### ISFR Data and Forest Key Terms

- Pages: 25
- Focus: State-wise forest cover rankings, mangrove cover changes, NDC carbon stock targets, and definitions of key forest-related terms.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Top 3 states by forest area: Madhya Pradesh, Arunachal Pradesh, Chhattisgarh.
- Top 3 states by forest percentage: Lakshadweep (91.33%), Mizoram, Andaman & Nicobar Islands.
- Total Mangrove cover decreased by 7.43 sq km to 4,992 sq km (Gujarat decreased; AP and Maharashtra increased).
- Forest Cover requires >= 1 hectare area and >= 10% tree canopy, irrespective of ownership.
- Tree cover comprises patches < 1 hectare outside recorded forest areas.

#### Section Excerpt

Details the top states in forest cover by area (Madhya Pradesh, Arunachal Pradesh, Chhattisgarh) and percentage (Lakshadweep, Mizoram, A&N Islands). Notes a decrease in total mangrove cover to 4,992 sq km. Highlights India's NDC target progress in carbon stock. Defines key terms including Forest Area, Forest Cover (>= 1 hectare, >= 10% canopy), Tree Cover, Carbon Stock, and Protected Forests.

#### Claims

- Madhya Pradesh has the largest forest cover by area in India, followed by Arunachal Pradesh and Chhattisgarh.
- Lakshadweep has the highest forest cover as a percentage of its geographical area, followed by Mizoram and the Andaman & Nicobar Islands.
- India's total mangrove cover decreased to 4,992 sq km, with Gujarat experiencing a decrease while Andhra Pradesh and Maharashtra saw an increase.
- Forest Cover is defined as all lands with a tree canopy density of 10% or more and an area of 1 hectare or more, irrespective of land ownership.
- Tree cover consists of tree patches smaller than 1 hectare that are located outside of recorded forest areas.

### Ramsar Convention on Wetlands

- Pages: 26

- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Ramsar CoP15 was held in Victoria Falls, Zimbabwe with the theme 'Protecting Wetlands for Our Common Future'.
- 'Wise use' means maintaining ecological character using ecosystem-based approaches within sustainable development.
- Adopted in 1971 in Ramsar, Iran; entered into force in 1975; India joined in 1982.
- Secretariat is located in Gland, Switzerland.

#### Section Excerpt

Summarizes the Ramsar Convention on Wetlands following its 15th Conference of Parties (CoP15) in Zimbabwe. It defines the 'wise use' of wetlands, notes its genesis in 1971 (entered into force in 1975), and highlights its 172 member countries (India joined in 1982) with a secretariat in Gland, Switzerland.

#### Claims

- The 15th Conference of Parties (CoP15) to the Ramsar Convention on Wetlands was held in Victoria Falls, Zimbabwe.
- Under the Ramsar Convention, the 'wise use' of wetlands is defined as maintaining their ecological character using ecosystem-based approaches within sustainable development.
- The Ramsar Convention was adopted in 1971 and entered into force in 1975, with India officially joining as a member in 1982.
- The Secretariat of the Ramsar Convention on Wetlands is located in Gland, Switzerland.

### Mechanisms under Ramsar Convention

- Pages: 27
- Focus: Ramsar List criteria, Montreux Record status in India, and Wetland City Accreditation details.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- India has 96 Ramsar sites, the highest number in Asia.
- The Montreux Record currently includes India's Keoladeo National Park and Loktak Lake; Chilika Lake was removed after successful conservation.
- Wetland City Accreditation (WCA) was approved at COP12 in 2015 and remains valid for 6 years before requiring renewal.

#### Section Excerpt

Details the Ramsar List, noting the UK has the most sites, Brazil's Rio Negro is the largest, and India has 96 (highest in Asia). The Montreux Record lists wetlands facing ecological change due to human interference; in India, it includes Keoladeo National Park and Loktak Lake, while Chilika Lake was removed. Wetland City Accreditation (WCA) is a voluntary system approved at COP12 (2015) that is valid for 6 years.

#### Claims

- India has 96 Ramsar sites, which is the highest number of such sites in Asia.
- The Montreux Record is a register of wetland sites where changes in ecological character have occurred, are occurring, or are likely to occur as a result of human interference.
- Keoladeo National Park and Loktak Lake are currently included in the Montreux Record, whereas Chilika Lake was removed from it after successful conservation.
- The Wetland City Accreditation (WCA) is a voluntary system approved at COP12 in 2015 that remains valid for 6 years before requiring renewal.
- Globally, the United Kingdom has the highest number of Ramsar sites, while Brazil's Rio Negro is the largest Ramsar site.

### New Ramsar Sites in India

- Pages: 28
- Focus: Newly designated Ramsar sites in India, their geographical locations, and specific ecological or historical details.

#### Evidence Bullets

- Khichan Wetland in Rajasthan's Thar Desert hosts wintering flocks of migratory demoiselle cranes.

- Siliserh Lake in Rajasthan is a human-made wetland located in the buffer region of the Sariska tiger reserve.
- Other newly listed sites include Udaipur Lake, Gogabeel Lake, and Gokul Reservoir in Bihar, and Kopra Jalashay in Chhattisgarh.

Lists new Ramsar sites including Khichan, Siliserh, and Menar in Rajasthan, along with sites in Bihar and Chhattisgarh. Khichan Wetland hosts wintering demoiselle cranes. Menar Wetland Complex is a freshwater monsoon wetland hosting critically endangered vultures. Siliserh Lake is a human-made wetland in the Sariska tiger reserve buffer zone, created in 1845 on a tributary of River Ruparel.

#### Claims

- Khichan Wetland is located in Rajasthan's Thar Desert and hosts wintering flocks of migratory demoiselle cranes.
- Menar Wetland Complex is a freshwater monsoon wetland in Rajasthan that hosts critically endangered white-rumped and long-billed vultures.
- Siliserh Lake is a human-made wetland situated in the buffer region of the Sariska tiger reserve in Rajasthan.
- Siliserh Lake was created on a tributary of the River Ruparel.
- Udaipur Lake, Gogabeel Lake, and Gokul Reservoir are Ramsar sites located in Bihar.
- Kopra Jalashay is a Ramsar site located in Chhattisgarh.

## Indian Wetlands and Coral Reef Conservation

- Topic: Wetlands and Corals
- Pages: 29-30
- Rationale: Covers major Indian lakes (Vembanad, Dal) and the distribution and conservation technologies for coral reefs.

### Coral Reefs and Coral Bleaching

- Pages: 29-30
- Focus: Coral biology, optimal growth conditions, bleaching mechanisms, conservation technologies, and global/Indian distribution.
- Question quota hint: 5
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Corals have a symbiotic relationship with zooxanthellae algae; unlike hard corals, soft corals lack calcium carbonate skeletons.
- Optimal coral conditions include 23-29°C temperatures, clear shallow waters less than 50 meters deep, and stable salinity.
- Biorock Technology uses electrical currents to stimulate coral formation via the deposition of calcium carbonate.
- India's largest coral reef system is located in the Andaman and Nicobar Group of Islands.
- The Coral Triangle includes the Philippines, Brunei, Malaysia, Singapore, Indonesia, Papua New Guinea, Timor-Leste, Solomon Islands, and Australia.

#### Section Excerpt

Details the symbiotic relationship between polyps and zooxanthellae, types of reefs (fringing, barrier, atoll), and optimal conditions (23-29°C, shallow clear water). Explains coral bleaching due to stress and highlights conservation technologies like Biorock and 3D-printed reefs. Maps distribution globally (Coral Triangle) and in India (Andaman & Nicobar, Lakshadweep, Gulf of Kachchh/Mannar).

#### Claims

- Soft corals differ from hard corals because they lack a calcium carbonate skeleton, although corals generally share a symbiotic relationship with zooxanthellae algae.
- Optimal conditions for coral reef growth include water temperatures between 23-29°C, clear shallow waters less than 50 meters deep, and stable salinity.
- Biorock Technology is a conservation method that uses electrical currents to stimulate coral formation through the deposition of calcium carbonate.
- The Andaman and Nicobar Group of Islands contains the largest coral reef system in India.
- The Coral Triangle is a marine area that includes the Philippines, Brunei, Malaysia, Singapore, Indonesia, Papua New Guinea, Timor-Leste, Solomon Islands, and Australia.

### Wetlands in News

- Pages: 29
- Focus: Key facts, geographical locations, and ecological significance of recently highlighted wetlands in India.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Gokul Reservoir (Buxar, Bihar) is an oxbow lake and an important habitat for Blackbuck.
- Gogabeel Lake (Katihar, Bihar) is the 1st community reserve of Bihar, formed from the flow of Mahananda, Kankhar, and Ganga rivers.
- Vembanad Lake (Kerala) is the largest brackish, humid tropical wetland ecosystem in Kerala, and its surface area shrank by 27% between 1917 and 1990.
- Dal Lake (J&K) hosted the first Khelo India water games, is fed by the Arrah river, and is divided into Gagribal, Lokut Dal, Bod Dal, and Nagin basins.

#### Section Excerpt

Covers Gokul Reservoir, Udaipur Lake, and Gogabeel Lake in Bihar (oxbow lakes, habitats for blackbuck and migratory birds, community reserves); Kopra Jalashay in Chhattisgarh; Vembanad lake in Kerala (largest brackish wetland, shrinking surface area); and Dal Lake in J&K (hosted Khelo India water games, fed by Arrah river).

#### Claims

- Gokul Reservoir is an oxbow lake located in Bihar and serves as an important habitat for the Blackbuck.
- Gogabeel Lake is the first community reserve of Bihar and is formed from the flow of the Mahananda, Kankhar, and Ganga rivers.
- Vembanad Lake is the largest brackish, humid tropical wetland ecosystem in Kerala.
- Dal Lake in Jammu and Kashmir is fed by the Arrah river and is divided into Gagribal, Lokut Dal, Bod Dal, and Nagin basins.

### Mangrove Ecosystems and SAIME Initiative

- Pages: 30
- Focus: The SAIME model for sustainable aquaculture, mangrove ecological characteristics, and mangrove distribution in India.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- SAIME is a multi-stakeholder partnership promoting sustainable shrimp trade to protect mangrove ecosystems in South Asia, specifically operationalizing Integrated Mangrove Aquaculture (IMA) in the Sunderbans.
- Mangroves feature unique root systems like pneumatophores and stilt roots, and exhibit vivipary where seeds germinate on maternal plants.
- Mangroves act as Nature's Carbon Vault, storing 7.5 to 10 times more carbon per acre than tropical forests.
- India recorded an 11.4% net increase in mangrove cover between 2001 and 2023.
- West Bengal holds the largest share of India's mangroves (42.45%), followed by Gujarat (23.32%) and the Andaman & Nicobar Islands (12.39%).

#### Section Excerpt

Discusses the Sustainable Aquaculture in Mangrove Ecosystems (SAIME) model recognized by FAO, aimed at protecting mangroves in South Asia (Sunderbans) through low-density shrimp farming. Details mangrove characteristics (pneumatophores, vivipary, carbon storage) and notes India's mangrove cover (4,991.68 sq. km) with West Bengal holding the largest share.

#### Claims

- The Sustainable Aquaculture in Mangrove Ecosystems (SAIME) initiative promotes sustainable shrimp trade to protect mangrove ecosystems, specifically operationalizing Integrated Mangrove Aquaculture in the Sunderbans.
- Mangroves exhibit vivipary, a reproductive adaptation where seeds germinate while still attached to the maternal plant.
- Mangroves act as highly efficient carbon sinks, storing 7.5 to 10 times more carbon per acre than tropical forests.
- India recorded an 11.4 percent net increase in its mangrove cover between 2001 and 2023.
- West Bengal holds the largest share of India's mangrove cover, followed by Gujarat and the Andaman and Nicobar Islands.

## Conservation Reserves and Environmental DNA

- Topic: Biodiversity Concepts
- Pages: 33-34
- Rationale: Explains the legal status of Conservation Reserves (Hesaraghatta) and emerging concepts like eDNA, Blue Washing, and De-extinction.

### Environmental DNA (eDNA)

- Pages: 33-34
- Focus: Definition, applications, and significance of eDNA in biodiversity conservation.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 3

#### Evidence Bullets

- eDNA is genetic material left by organisms in environments like water, soil, or air.
- It is used for mapping species presence, distribution, and tracking ecosystems.
- It is a non-invasive, highly sensitive, rapid, and cost-effective biomonitoring method compared to traditional methods.

#### Section Excerpt

Scientists are now focusing on modern tools like environmental DNA (eDNA) analysis for biodiversity conservation. It is the genetic material, like DNA from cells, tissues, fluids, and excrement, left by organisms in the environment (e.g., water, soil, Air, etc.). Application: Mapping species presence and distribution, tracking Ecosystems, etc. Significance: Non-invasive, highly sensitive, rapid and cost-effective.

#### Claims

- Environmental DNA (eDNA) consists of genetic material left by organisms in environments such as water, soil, or air through cells, tissues, fluids, and excrement.
- eDNA analysis is applied in biodiversity conservation for mapping species presence, determining species distribution, and tracking ecosystems.
- eDNA analysis is a non-invasive, highly sensitive, rapid, and cost-effective biomonitoring method compared to traditional methods.
