### Conservation Reserves and Hesaraghatta Grassland

- Pages: 33
- Focus: Legal status of Conservation Reserves under WPA 1972 and details of Hesaraghatta Grassland.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Conservation Reserves are declared under Section 36A of the Wildlife Protection Act (WPA) 1972.
- They act as buffer zones or migration corridors between established national parks, sanctuaries, and forests.
- A Conservation Reserve Management Committee advises the Chief Wild Life Warden.
- Hesaraghatta Grassland is the last remaining grassland habitat in the Bengaluru region.
- It forms a large catchment for the Arkavathy River and Thippagondanahalli reservoir.

#### Section Excerpt

State Government has notified Hesaraghatta Grassland Conservation Reserve under Section 36A of the Wildlife Protection Act (WPA) 1972. Considered a protected area under WPA 1972 and can be declared by State Governments and Central Government... Typically act as buffer zones to or connectors and migration corridors... Hesaraghatta Grassland Location: Around Hesaraghatta Lake near Bengaluru in Karnataka and is the last remaining grassland habitat in Bengaluru region. Large catchment of the Arkavathy River.

#### Claims

- Conservation Reserves are notified under Section 36A of the Wildlife Protection Act (WPA) 1972.
- Conservation Reserves typically act as buffer zones or migration corridors between established national parks, sanctuaries, and forests.
- A Conservation Reserve Management Committee advises the Chief Wild Life Warden on the management of the reserve.
- Hesaraghatta Grassland is the last remaining grassland habitat in the Bengaluru region of Karnataka.
- Hesaraghatta Grassland forms a large catchment area for the Arkavathy River and Thippagondanahalli reservoir.

### Global Ocean Darkening

- Pages: 33
- Focus: Causes, mechanisms, and impacts of reduced light penetration in global oceans.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- 21% of the global ocean became darker between 2003 and 2022, notably in the Arctic, Antarctic, and Gulf Stream.
- Ocean darkening shrinks the photic zone, which is the sunlit layer up to 200 meters deep where 90% of marine life thrives.
- Coastal darkening is caused by nutrient, organic material, and sediment loading from agricultural runoff.
- Open ocean darkening is driven by surface warming leading to algal blooms and climate-driven circulation changes.
- Impacts include limited ocean productivity, declining fish stocks, and affected climate regulation.

#### Section Excerpt

A study found that 21% of the global ocean had become darker between 2003 and 2022, especially in the Arctic, Antarctic, and Gulf Stream region. It refers to a reduction in light penetration into the global oceans, shrinking the photic zone. Reasons: In Coastal Oceans: Combination of nutrient, organic material and sediment loading... In Open Oceans: warming of the surface oceans (leading to algal bloom)... Impact: Limits Ocean productivity.

#### Claims

- Ocean darkening refers to the reduction in light penetration into the oceans, which shrinks the photic zone (the sunlit layer up to 200 meters deep where 90% of marine life thrives).
- Coastal ocean darkening is primarily caused by nutrient, organic material, and sediment loading from agricultural runoff, whereas open ocean darkening is driven by surface warming that leads to algal blooms.
- Between 2003 and 2022, 21% of the global ocean became darker, with notable occurrences in the Arctic, Antarctic, and Gulf Stream regions.
- The impacts of global ocean darkening include limited ocean productivity, declining fish stocks, and disruptions to climate regulation.

### Blue Washing and Related Terms

- Pages: 34
- Focus: Deceptive marketing tactics including blue washing, greenwashing, and pinkwashing.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 3

#### Evidence Bullets

- Blue washing overstates a company's commitment to responsible, sustainable, social, and ethical business practices.
- Greenwashing involves making false or misleading claims about environmental efforts to appear eco-friendly.
- Pinkwashing uses LGBTQ+ rights as a marketing tool while failing to improve actual conditions for LGBTQ+ employees.

#### Section Excerpt

To counter 'greenwashing' allegations against it, Waste to energy (WTE) industry seems to have adapted 'blue washing'. About Blue Washing: It is a deceptive marketing tactic that overstates a company's commitment to responsible and sustainable social and ethical business practices. Greenwashing: Making false or misleading claims about a company's environmental efforts... Pinkwashing: Using LGBTQ+ rights as a marketing tool.

#### Claims

- Blue washing is a deceptive marketing tactic where a company overstates its commitment to responsible, sustainable, social, and ethical business practices.
- Greenwashing involves making false or misleading claims specifically about a company's environmental efforts to appear eco-friendly.
- Pinkwashing is the practice of using LGBTQ+ rights as a marketing tool while failing to improve actual conditions for LGBTQ+ employees.

### Functional De-extinction

- Pages: 34
- Focus: Concept, techniques, and examples of reviving ecological roles of extinct species.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Functional de-extinction focuses on reviving the ecological roles and traits of extinct species rather than creating exact replicas.
- Techniques include CRISPR gene editing, cloning, and genome mapping.
- Colossal Biosciences engineered grey wolf pups to carry traits of the extinct dire wolf.
- Other planned candidates for functional de-extinction include the wooly mammoth, moa, thylacine, and dodo.

#### Section Excerpt

A US biotech company, Colossal Biosciences, claims to have genetically engineered three grey wolf pups to carry traits of the extinct dire wolf, calling it world's first successful functional de-extinction. It focuses on reviving ecological roles and traits of extinct species. Revived organisms are not exact replicas but genetically engineered to closely resemble extinct species... Techniques used: CRISPR gene editing, cloning, and genome mapping.

#### Claims

- Functional de-extinction aims to revive the ecological roles and traits of extinct species rather than creating exact genetic replicas.
- Techniques utilized in functional de-extinction include CRISPR gene editing, cloning, and genome mapping.
- The first successful functional de-extinction involved genetically engineering grey wolf pups to carry traits of the extinct dire wolf.
- Planned candidates for functional de-extinction include the wooly mammoth, moa, thylacine, and dodo.

### Ungulates

- Pages: 34
- Focus: Characteristics, habitat, and ecological significance of hoofed mammals in India.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Ungulates are hoofed mammals, primarily herbivores, including chital, antelopes, sambar, and nilgai.
- WII and NTCA conducted India's first survey of ungulates, noting declines in Odisha, Jharkhand, and Chhattisgarh.
- They serve as key prey species for tigers.
- They aid in forest and grassland regeneration through seed dispersal, nutrient cycling, and maintaining soil health.

#### Section Excerpt

Wildlife Institute of India and National Tiger Conservation Authority did India's first survey of ungulates (hoofed animals), warning of their decline in Odisha, Jharkhand, and Chhattisgarh. Characteristics: Primarily herbivores mammals including chital, antelopes, sambar, nilgai, etc.; Prey species for tigers. Significance: Aid forest/grassland regeneration through seed dispersal; nutrient cycling; soil health.

#### Claims

- Ungulates are hoofed mammals that are primarily herbivores, including species such as chital, antelopes, sambar, and nilgai.
- The Wildlife Institute of India and the National Tiger Conservation Authority conducted India's first survey of ungulates.
- India's first survey of ungulates warned of a decline in their populations in Odisha, Jharkhand, and Chhattisgarh.
- Ungulates aid in forest and grassland regeneration through seed dispersal, nutrient cycling, and maintaining soil health.

## CITES and National Green Tribunal

- Topic: Environmental Organizations
- Pages: 35-36
- Rationale: Focuses on the legal structure and appendices of CITES and the statutory powers and legislative framework of the NGT.

### Convention on International Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CITES)

- Pages: 35
- Focus: International agreement regulating wildlife trade, its appendices, and key initiatives.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- CITES is legally binding on parties but does not replace national laws.
- India ratified CITES in 1976.
- Appendix I covers species threatened with extinction; Appendix II covers species needing trade control; Appendix III covers species protected in at least one country asking for assistance.
- Key initiatives include the MIKE Programme, CITES Tree Species Programme, and ICCWC.
- CITES publishes the World Wildlife Trade Report.

#### Section Excerpt

Voluntary international agreement between governments ensuring international trade in wild animals and plants does not threaten their survival. Legally binding on Parties, but does not take the place of national laws. 185 parties (including India - ratified in 1976). CITES Three Appendices: Based on degree of protection needed. Key Initiatives of CITES: MIKE Programme to monitor illegal killing of elephants; CITES Tree Species Programme (CTSP); International Consortium on Combating Wildlife Crime (ICCWC), 2010.

#### Claims

- CITES is a legally binding agreement on its parties but does not take the place of national laws.
- India is a party to CITES, having ratified the convention in 1976.
- CITES categorizes species into three Appendices based on the degree of protection needed, with Appendix I specifically covering species threatened with extinction.
- The MIKE Programme, CITES Tree Species Programme (CTSP), and the International Consortium on Combating Wildlife Crime (ICCWC) are key initiatives associated with CITES.
- The World Wildlife Trade Report is published by CITES.

### International Union for the Protection of New Varieties of Plants (UPOV)

- Pages: 35
- Focus: Plant Variety Protection, Breeder's Rights, and India's non-member status.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Established in 1961 to promote Plant Variety Protection (PVP) and grant Breeder's Rights.
- UPOV Convention 1991 strengthens breeders' rights and makes farmer's privilege to save seeds optional.
- India is not a member of UPOV.
- India uses the Protection of Plant Varieties and Farmers' Rights Authority Act, 2001, uniquely protecting both breeder and farmer's rights.

#### Section Excerpt

An intergovernmental organization to provide and promote an effective system of Plant Variety Protection (PVP). Grants Breeder's Rights (an IP right) to incentivize the development of new, improved plant varieties. UPOV Convention 1991: It strengthens breeders' rights and makes the farmer's privilege (to save seeds) optional for member countries. India's Status: Not a member and uses its own sui generis law, the Protection of Plant Varieties and Farmers' Rights Authority Act, 2001.

#### Claims

- The International Union for the Protection of New Varieties of Plants (UPOV) is an intergovernmental organization that grants Breeder's Rights as an intellectual property right.
- The UPOV Convention of 1991 makes the farmer's privilege to save seeds optional for its member countries.
- India is not a member of the International Union for the Protection of New Varieties of Plants (UPOV).
- India uses a sui generis law, the Protection of Plant Varieties and Farmers' Rights Authority Act, 2001, to protect plant varieties instead of the UPOV framework.

### Integrated Biodiversity Assessment Tool (IBAT) Alliance

- Pages: 36
- Focus: Coalition of organizations providing biodiversity data and tools.
- Question quota hint: 1
- Claim count: 2

#### Evidence Bullets

- IBAT is a coalition of Birdlife International, Conservation International, IUCN, and UNEP-WCMC.
- Founded in 2008 to provide data and tools for biodiversity-related risks.

#### Section Excerpt

A coalition of four organizations: Birdlife International, Conservation International, The International Union of Conservation for Nature (IUCN), and The United Nations Environment Programme - World Conservation Monitoring Centre (UNEP-WCMC). Founded in 2008. Mission: To provide data, tools and guidance that help organisations act on biodiversity-related risks.

#### Claims

- The Integrated Biodiversity Assessment Tool (IBAT) Alliance is a coalition comprising Birdlife International, Conservation International, IUCN, and UNEP-WCMC.
- The mission of the IBAT Alliance, founded in 2008, is to provide data, tools, and guidance to help organizations act on biodiversity-related risks.

### National Green Tribunal (NGT)

- Pages: 36
- Focus: Statutory framework, jurisdiction, powers, and governance of the NGT.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Established in 2010 under the NGT Act as a statutory body for environmental cases.
- Principal sitting place is New Delhi, with four others: Bhopal, Pune, Kolkata, Chennai.
- Jurisdiction covers 7 Acts including Water Act, Forest Act, Air Act, EPA 1986, Public Liability Insurance Act, and Biological Diversity Act.
- Has powers of a Civil Court but is guided by Principles of Natural Justice and not bound by the Code of Civil Procedure or Indian Evidence Act.
- Members hold office for 5 years and are not eligible for re-appointment.
- Appeals against NGT orders can be made to the Supreme Court within 90 days.

#### Section Excerpt

A Statutory and specialized body aimed at effective and expeditious disposal of cases relating to environmental protection. Established in 2010 under the NGT Act. Principal Place of Sitting: New Delhi. Legislative Framework: Water Act, Forest Act, Air Act, Environment (Protection) Act, Public Liability Insurance Act, Biological Diversity Act. Powers: Has power of a Civil Court... Guided by Principles of Natural Justice. Members: Hold office for 5 years, but shall not be eligible for re-appointment. Appeal: Orders are binding but not final (appeal could be made in SC within ninety days).

#### Claims

- The National Green Tribunal (NGT) is a statutory body established in 2010 under the NGT Act.
- The jurisdiction of the NGT covers seven specific environmental laws, including the Water Act, Forest Act, Air Act, Environment (Protection) Act, Public Liability Insurance Act, and Biological Diversity Act.
- The NGT is guided by the Principles of Natural Justice and is not bound by the Code of Civil Procedure or the Indian Evidence Act.
- Members of the NGT hold office for a term of 5 years and are not eligible for re-appointment.
- Appeals against NGT orders can be made directly to the Supreme Court within 90 days.

## Global Forest Assessment and UNFCCC COP30

- Topic: Climate Change
- Pages: 37-38
- Rationale: Covers the FAO's GFRA 2025 and the foundational outcomes of the UNFCCC COP30 (Belem Package).

### Global Forest Resources Assessment (GFRA) 2025

- Pages: 37
- Focus: Key findings of the GFRA 2025, FAO's role, and India's global rankings in forest cover and rubber plantations.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- The Global Forest Resources Assessment (GFRA) is released by the FAO every 5 years.
- The Global Forest Observations Initiative (GFOI) is a flagship programme of the Group on Earth Observations (GEO), of which India is a member.
- Forests cover 32% of the global land area, with nearly half located in the tropics.
- Europe has the largest forest area, accounting for 25% of the world's total.
- India ranks 9th globally in total forest area (accounting for 2% of global forest area) and 5th in rubber plantations.

#### Section Excerpt

Global Forest Resources Assessment (GFRA) 2025 Released by: FAO, every 5 years. This year's edition published during Global Forest Observations Initiative (GFOI) Plenary in Bali, Indonesia. GFOI is a flagship programme of the Group on Earth Observations (GEO) (India is a member). Key highlights: Forests cover 32% of global land area. Nearly half of the world's forests are located in tropics... Europe has the largest forest area, accounting for 25% of world's total. India's Forest Extent: Moved up one rank to 9th position in terms of total forest area globally, accounting for 2% of global forest area; Ranks 5th in terms of rubber plantation.

#### Claims

- The Global Forest Resources Assessment (GFRA) is released by the Food and Agriculture Organization (FAO) every five years.
- The Global Forest Observations Initiative (GFOI) is a flagship programme of the Group on Earth Observations (GEO), of which India is a member.
- Forests cover 32 percent of the global land area, with nearly half of the world's forests located in the tropics.
- Europe has the largest forest area globally, accounting for 25 percent of the world's total forest area.
- India ranks 9th globally in total forest area, accounting for 2 percent of the global forest area.
- India ranks 5th globally in terms of rubber plantations.

### COP30 Outcomes and Initiatives

- Pages: 38
- Focus: Specific initiatives launched at COP30, including the Belem Mission, Global Implementation Accelerator, NAPs Alliance, and TFFF.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- The Belem Mission to 1.5 C was launched by the COP29-COP31 Troika (UAE, Azerbaijan, Brazil) as an action-oriented platform.
- The Global Implementation Accelerator is a voluntary initiative by COP 30 & 31 Presidencies to support NDC and NAP implementation.
- The NAPs Implementation Alliance was launched by UNDP, Italy, Germany, NAP Global Network, and NDC Partnership to mobilize investment for national adaptation priorities.
- The Tropical Forest Forever Facility (TFFF) was endorsed by Brazil, Indonesia, DRC, and China to establish a $125B Tropical Forest Investment Fund (TFIF).

#### Section Excerpt

COP30 Outcomes and Initiatives. Implementation Work Programme (Mutirao Decision). Belem Mission to 1.5 C Launched By/Under: COP29-COP31 Troika (UAE, Azerbaijan, Brazil)... Global Implementation Accelerator Launched By/Under: COP 30 & 31 Presidencies... National Adaptation Plans (NAPs) Implementation Alliance Launched By/Under: UNDP, Italy, Germany, NAP Global Network, NDC Partnership... Forests & Nature. Tropical Forest Forever Facility (TFFF) Launched By/Under: Endorsed by Brazil, Indonesia, DRC, China. Primary Purpose: Establish a $125B fund Tropical Forest Investment Fund (TFIF) that pays tropical forest countries for maintaining and expanding forest cover.

#### Claims

- The Belem Mission to 1.5 C was launched by the COP29-COP31 Troika, which comprises the UAE, Azerbaijan, and Brazil.
- The Global Implementation Accelerator is a voluntary initiative launched by the COP 30 and 31 Presidencies to support the implementation of Nationally Determined Contributions (NDCs) and National Adaptation Plans (NAPs).
- The National Adaptation Plans (NAPs) Implementation Alliance was launched by a coalition including the UNDP, Italy, Germany, the NAP Global Network, and the NDC Partnership to mobilize investment for adaptation priorities.
- The Tropical Forest Forever Facility (TFFF) aims to establish a $125 billion Tropical Forest Investment Fund (TFIF) to compensate tropical forest countries for maintaining and expanding their forest cover.
- The Tropical Forest Forever Facility (TFFF) was endorsed by Brazil, Indonesia, the Democratic Republic of Congo (DRC), and China.

### UNFCCC COP30 Overview and Background

- Pages: 38
- Focus: Details of the COP30 summit in Belem, the Belem Package, and foundational facts about the UNFCCC.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- COP30 was held in Belem, Brazil, coinciding with the 10th anniversary of the Paris Agreement.
- The summit concluded with the adoption of the Belem Package by 195 Parties.
- UNFCCC emerged from the 1992 Earth Summit at Rio de Janeiro and is one of the three Rio Conventions (along with UNCBD and UNCCD).
- UNFCCC has 198 signatories (including India) and is headquartered in Bonn, Germany.
- The UNFCCC releases the NDC Synthesis Report.

#### Section Excerpt

The 30th Conference of Parties (COP30) to United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC) was held in Belem, Brazil which coincided with the 10th anniversary of the Paris Agreement. Hosted in the Amazon region, the summit was framed as the 'COP of Implementation', 'COP of Truth', and the 'Forest COP', rooted in the 'spirit of Mutirao' (inclusion and balance). It concluded with adoption of Belem Package, by 195 Parties... United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC) Genesis: It emerged from 1992 Earth Summit at Rio de Janeiro, adopted in 1994. It is one of the three sister conventions (Rio Conventions)... Signatories: 198 (India is a member). Report: NDC Synthesis Report. Headquarters: Bonn, Germany.

#### Claims

- The 30th Conference of Parties (COP30) to the UNFCCC was held in Belem, Brazil, and coincided with the 10th anniversary of the Paris Agreement.
- The UNFCCC COP30 summit concluded with the adoption of the Belem Package.
- The UNFCCC emerged from the 1992 Earth Summit at Rio de Janeiro and is one of the three Rio Conventions.
- The UNFCCC is responsible for releasing the Nationally Determined Contributions (NDC) Synthesis Report.
- The UNFCCC is headquartered in Bonn, Germany, and India is a member state.

## COP30 Finance and International Initiatives

- Topic: Climate Change
- Pages: 39-40
- Rationale: Details specific COP30 finance mechanisms (TFFF, RAIZ) and international declarations like the Belem Declaration for Green Industrialization.

### Climate Finance and Adaptation Frameworks

- Pages: 39
- Focus: Baku to Belém Roadmap, NCQG, and Global Goal on Adaptation (GGA) frameworks
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Baku to Belém Roadmap to 1.3T is the official pathway to mobilize the NCQG of $1.3 trillion/year by 2035.
- The framework targets tripling adaptation finance to $120 billion per year.
- The GGA measurement framework adopted indicators across 7 thematic sectors: water, food, health, ecosystems, infrastructure, poverty, and culture.
- The Global Goal on Adaptation (GGA) was established under Article 7.1 of the Paris Agreement (2015).
- Baku Adaptation Roadmap establishes adaptation work for 2026-2028 until the next Global Stocktake.

#### Section Excerpt

Baku to Belém Roadmap to 1.3T: The official pathway to mobilize the New Collective Quantified Goal (NCQG) of $1.3 trillion/year by 2035. Tripling of adaptation finance: Increase adaptation finance to $120 billion per year. Measurement framework for tracking adaptation progress under the Global Goal on Adaptation (GGA): Indicators adopted across 7 thematic sectors. Formalizes a four-stage cycle. Global Goal on Adaptation (GGA) was established under Article 7.1 of the Paris Agreement (2015). Baku Adaptation Roadmap: Approves and establishes the work for 2026-2028, until the next Global Stocktake of Paris Agreement.

#### Claims

- The Baku to Belém Roadmap to 1.3T is the official pathway to mobilize the New Collective Quantified Goal (NCQG) of $1.3 trillion per year by 2035.
- The climate finance framework targets the tripling of adaptation finance to $120 billion per year.
- The Global Goal on Adaptation (GGA) measurement framework tracks progress using indicators across 7 thematic sectors and formalizes a four-stage cycle.
- The Global Goal on Adaptation (GGA) was established under Article 7.1 of the Paris Agreement (2015).
- The Baku Adaptation Roadmap establishes adaptation work for the period 2026-2028, leading up to the next Global Stocktake of the Paris Agreement.

### Forest and Agriculture Finance Initiatives

- Pages: 39
- Focus: Tropical Forest Forever Facility (TFFF), RAIZ Accelerator, and Scaling J-REDD+ Coalition
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- TFFF aims to mobilize $25 billion with the World Bank as trustee and India participating as an observer.
- TFFF mandates that at least 20% of all payments flow directly to Indigenous Peoples and Local Communities.
- RAIZ Accelerator aims to restore 3 million hectares of degraded farmland and is hosted by Brazil under the FAO FAST Partnership.
- Scaling J-REDD+ Coalition aims to mobilize $3-6 billion per year by 2030 to halt and reverse tropical deforestation.

#### Section Excerpt

Tropical Forest Forever Facility (TFFF): India joined as an Observer. Trustee and Interim host: World Bank. Aims to mobilise $25 billion from member countries and attract up to $100 billion from private investors. Mandates at least 20% of all payments flow directly to Indigenous Peoples and Local Communities. Resilient Agriculture Investment for net-Zero land degradation (RAIZ) Accelerator: Brazil & 10 Partner Nations. Hosted by Brazil under the FAO FAST Partnership. To restore 3 million hectares of degraded farmland. Scaling J-REDD+ Coalition: Offers a credible, mature pathway to mobilize $3-6 billion per year by 2030 to halt and reverse tropical deforestation.

#### Claims

- India joined the Tropical Forest Forever Facility (TFFF) as an Observer, with the World Bank acting as its trustee and interim host.
- The Tropical Forest Forever Facility (TFFF) mandates that at least 20% of all its payments flow directly to Indigenous Peoples and Local Communities.
- The Resilient Agriculture Investment for net-Zero land degradation (RAIZ) Accelerator is hosted by Brazil under the FAO FAST Partnership and aims to restore 3 million hectares of degraded farmland.
- The Scaling J-REDD+ Coalition aims to mobilize $3-6 billion per year by 2030 to halt and reverse tropical deforestation.

Plan to Accelerate Coal Transitions (PACT): Supported by Powering Past Coal Alliance (PPCA) to fast-track the transition from coal to clean energy. Belém 4x Pledge on Sustainable Fuels: Participants include Italy, Japan, India and Brazil. Objective is to quadruple the use of sustainable fuels (hydrogen, biofuels, e-fuels) by 2035 compared to 2024 levels, with progress tracked by the IEA.

#### Claims

- The Plan to Accelerate the Expansion and Resilience of Power Grids is supported by the Green Grids Initiative (GGI).
- The Plan to Accelerate Coal Transitions (PACT) is supported by the Powering Past Coal Alliance (PPCA).
- The Belém 4x Pledge aims to quadruple the use of sustainable fuels, such as hydrogen and biofuels, by 2035 compared to 2024 levels.
- The progress of the Belém 4x Pledge on Sustainable Fuels will be tracked by the International Energy Agency (IEA).
- India is a participant in the Belém 4x Pledge on Sustainable Fuels.

Belém Gender Action Plan (GAP): Adopted by Parties at COP30 for 2026-2034 to implement Lima Work Programme on gender. Declaration on Information Integrity on Climate Change: Establishes shared international commitments to address climate disinformation. First time the topic of information integrity has been included in the COP Action Agenda. Belém Declaration on Hunger, Poverty, and People-Centered Climate Action: Signed by 43 countries and the EU to promote actions addressing unequal climate impacts.

#### Claims

- The Belém Gender Action Plan (GAP) was adopted at COP30 for the period 2026-2034 to implement the Lima Work Programme on gender.
- The Declaration on Information Integrity on Climate Change represents the first time the topic of information integrity has been included in the COP Action Agenda.
- The Belém Declaration on Hunger, Poverty, and People-Centered Climate Action was signed by 43 countries and the EU to address unequal climate impacts.

## COP30 Health, Oceans, and Methane Initiatives

### COP30 Emergency Actions and Key Outcomes

- Pages: 41
- Focus: Early warning systems, climate-health financing, heat resilience, and food waste reduction initiatives launched at COP30.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Systematic Observation Impact Bond is the world's first impact bond on weather and climate data, created by SOFF (UNEP, WMO, UNDP).
- Climate and Health Funders Coalition (CHFC) committed an initial $300 million focusing on extreme heat, air pollution, and infectious diseases.
- Global Mutirao Against Extreme Heat is led by the COP30 Presidency and UNEP-led Cool Coalition, joined by 185 cities.
- Food Waste Breakthrough is led by UNEP and targets halving global food waste and a 7% reduction in methane emissions by 2030.

#### Section Excerpt

Emergency Action includes the 2030 Strategy for Climate Risk and Early Warning Systems (CREWS) and Brazil's Call to Action on Integrated Fire Management. Other outcomes include the Systematic Observation Impact Bond by SOFF (UNEP, WMO, UNDP) to help LDCs and SIDS meet GBON standards. The Climate and Health Funders Coalition committed $300 million. The Global Mutirao Against Extreme Heat, led by the Cool Coalition, aims to reduce heat risks for 3 billion people, alongside the Global Cooling Pledge. The Food Waste Breakthrough, led by UNEP, targets halving global food waste and reducing methane emissions by 7% by 2030.

#### Claims

- The Global Mutirao Against Extreme Heat is an initiative led by the COP30 Presidency and the UNEP-led Cool Coalition aimed at reducing heat risks for 3 billion people.
- The Food Waste Breakthrough, led by UNEP, targets halving global food waste and achieving a 7% reduction in methane emissions by 2030.
- The Climate and Health Funders Coalition (CHFC) committed an initial $300 million to focus on extreme heat, air pollution, and infectious diseases.

### Country or Regional Platforms for Climate and Nature Finance

- Pages: 42
- Focus: The announcement by India and other developing nations to create climate finance platforms supported by the Green Climate Fund.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- India, 12 developing countries, and the AISCC announced plans for Country or Regional Platforms for Climate and Nature Finance at COP30.
- The platforms are supported by the Green Climate Fund's (GCF) Readiness Programme.
- The GCF Readiness Programme is the largest climate action capacity-building facility for developing countries.
- Existing platforms supported by GCF include the Brazil Country Platform and the Caribbean Regional Platform.

#### Section Excerpt

At COP30, India, along with 12 other developing countries and the African Islands States Climate Commission (AISCC), announced plans to develop 'Country or Regional Platforms for Climate and Nature Finance'. These are strategic, country-driven mechanisms translating climate priorities into programmatic investment approaches. They are supported under the Green Climate Fund's (GCF) Readiness Programme, which is the largest climate action capacity-building facility for developing countries. GCF already supports the Brazil Country Platform and the Caribbean Regional Platform.

#### Claims

- India, along with 12 other developing countries and the African Islands States Climate Commission (AISCC), announced plans to develop 'Country or Regional Platforms for Climate and Nature Finance' at COP30.
- The 'Country or Regional Platforms for Climate and Nature Finance' are supported under the Green Climate Fund's (GCF) Readiness Programme.
- The Green Climate Fund's (GCF) Readiness Programme is the largest climate action capacity-building facility for developing countries.
- The Green Climate Fund (GCF) already provides support to the Brazil Country Platform and the Caribbean Regional Platform.

## Green Climate Fund and India's NDCs

- Topic: Climate Change
- Pages: 43-44
- Rationale: Explains the GCF mandate and provides a detailed update on India's quantitative NDC targets for 2030.

### India's Nationally Determined Contributions (NDCs) and Panchamrit

- Pages: 43-44
- Focus: India's updated NDC targets for 2030, progress achieved, and the Panchamrit strategy announced at COP26.
- Question quota hint: 5
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- India's updated 2022 NDC targets a 45% reduction in emissions intensity of GDP from 2005 levels by 2030, with 36% already achieved between 2005 and 2020.
- India targets 50% cumulative electric power installed capacity from non-fossil fuel-based energy by 2030, and has achieved 51% as of September 2025.
- India's updated NDC includes the 'LiFE' (Lifestyle for Environment) mass movement component as a key to achieving its quantitative targets.
- Panchamrit commitments at COP26 Glasgow include reaching 500 GW non-fossil energy capacity by 2030 and achieving net-zero emissions by 2070.

#### Section Excerpt

Details India's updated 2022 NDCs, including reducing emissions intensity of GDP by 45% from 2005 levels, achieving 50% cumulative electric power installed capacity from non-fossil fuels, and creating an additional carbon sink of 2.5 to 3 billion tonnes. Also covers the Panchamrit commitments made at COP26 Glasgow, including the net-zero target by 2070.

#### Claims

- India's updated 2022 NDC targets a 45% reduction in the emissions intensity of its GDP from 2005 levels by 2030.
- India aims to achieve 50% cumulative electric power installed capacity from non-fossil fuel-based energy resources by 2030 as part of its updated NDCs.
- The 'LiFE' (Lifestyle for Environment) mass movement is formally included as a component of India's updated Nationally Determined Contributions.
- Under the Panchamrit strategy announced at COP26 Glasgow, India committed to achieving net-zero emissions by the year 2070.
- India's Panchamrit commitments include a target to reach 500 GW of non-fossil energy capacity by 2030.
- India's NDCs include the creation of an additional carbon sink of 2.5 to 3 billion tonnes.

### Global Climate Funds and Initiatives

- Pages: 43
- Focus: Key international climate funds and partnerships including GCF, ARISE, Blue NDC Challenge, and LeadIT.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Green Climate Fund (GCF) was established at COP 16 in Cancun (2010) and mandates a 50:50 investment split between mitigation and adaptation.
- ARISE is implemented through Climate Investment Funds (CIF), which comprises the Clean Technology Fund and Strategic Climate Fund.
- Blue NDC Challenge was launched by Brazil and France to place oceans at the heart of NDCs, recognizing oceans as carbon sinks that absorb up to 4 times more CO2 than terrestrial forests.
- Leadership Group for Industry Transition (LeadIT) was launched jointly by India and Sweden in 2019 to achieve net-zero carbon emissions from high-emitting industries by 2050.

#### Section Excerpt

Covers the Green Climate Fund (established COP 16, 50% mitigation/adaptation split), ARISE investment program (implemented through CIF), Blue NDC Challenge (launched by Brazil and France to integrate oceans into NDCs), and LeadIT (launched by India and Sweden for industry transition to net-zero by 2050).

#### Claims

- The Green Climate Fund (GCF) was established at COP 16 in Cancun and mandates a 50:50 investment split between mitigation and adaptation.
- The ARISE investment program is implemented through the Climate Investment Funds (CIF), which consists of the Clean Technology Fund and the Strategic Climate Fund.
- The Blue NDC Challenge, launched by Brazil and France, aims to integrate oceans into NDCs because oceans act as carbon sinks that absorb up to four times more CO2 than terrestrial forests.
- The Leadership Group for Industry Transition (LeadIT) was launched jointly by India and Sweden to achieve net-zero carbon emissions from high-emitting industries by 2050.

### Greenhouse Gases Emission Intensity (GEI) Target Rules, 2025

- Pages: 44
- Focus: The newly notified GEI Target Rules, obligated sectors, compliance mechanisms, and penalties.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- GEI Target Rules 2025 establish legally binding GHG emission targets for four primary sectors: aluminium, cement, chlor-alkali, and pulp & paper.
- GEI targets are calculated using the Bureau of Energy Efficiency's (BEE) methodology specific to each obligated entity.
- Obligated entities must meet targets annually under the Carbon Credit Trading Scheme, 2023, or purchase certificates from the Indian Carbon Market (ICM).
- Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) imposes environmental compensation equal to twice the average traded price of carbon credit certificates for non-compliance.

#### Section Excerpt

Outlines the GEI Target Rules 2025 notified by the Union Environment Ministry, proposing legally binding GHG emission targets for over 400 industrial units. It covers four primary energy-intensive sectors (aluminium, cement, chlor-alkali, pulp & paper) and five additional sectors. Compliance is linked to the Carbon Credit Trading Scheme, 2023, with CPCB imposing environmental compensation for shortfalls.

#### Claims

- The GEI Target Rules 2025 establish legally binding greenhouse gas emission targets for four primary energy-intensive sectors: aluminium, cement, chlor-alkali, and pulp & paper.
- Under the GEI Target Rules 2025, greenhouse gas emission intensity targets for obligated entities are calculated using a methodology developed by the Bureau of Energy Efficiency (BEE).
- Obligated entities under the GEI Target Rules 2025 can achieve compliance by meeting targets under the Carbon Credit Trading Scheme, 2023, or by purchasing certificates from the Indian Carbon Market (ICM).
- The Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) imposes an environmental compensation equal to twice the average traded price of carbon credit certificates on entities failing to comply with the GEI Target Rules 2025.

## Carbon Credit Trading and Green Credit Program

- Topic: Climate Change
- Pages: 45-46
- Rationale: Covers the CCTS under the Energy Conservation Act and the voluntary Green Credit Program linked to Mission LiFE.

### Green Credit Program (GCP) and Methodology

- Pages: 45-46
- Focus: Objectives, administration, and calculation methodology of the Green Credit Program under the Environment (Protection) Act, 1986.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Established under the Environment (Protection) Act, 1986, with MoEFCC as the nodal agency.
- Administered by the Indian Council of Forestry Research and Education (ICFRE), Dehradun.
- Supports the Mission LiFE (Lifestyle for Environment) initiative.
- Revised tree plantation methodology requires 5 years of restoration and a minimum 40% canopy density to claim credits.
- 1 Green Credit equals 1 new tree (over 5 years old), and these specific credits are non-tradable and non-transferable.
- Identified land parcels for the program must be at least five hectares in size.

#### Section Excerpt

The Green Credit Program is an innovative market-based mechanism established under the Environment (Protection) Act, 1986, to incentivize voluntary environmental actions like afforestation and water conservation. It supports the Mission LiFE initiative. The Indian Council of Forestry Research and Education (ICFRE) is the administering body. Under the revised methodology for tree plantation, 1 Green Credit equals 1 new tree (over 5 years old) on degraded forest land with a minimum 40% canopy density. These specific credits are non-tradable and non-transferable. Eligible land parcels must be at least five hectares.

#### Claims

- The Green Credit Program (GCP) is established under the Environment (Protection) Act, 1986.
- The Indian Council of Forestry Research and Education (ICFRE) is the administering body for the Green Credit Program.
- Under the revised tree plantation methodology, one Green Credit is equivalent to one new tree over 5 years old on degraded forest land with a minimum 40% canopy density.
- Green Credits generated under the specific revised tree plantation methodology are non-tradable and non-transferable.
- Eligible land parcels for the Green Credit Program must be at least five hectares in size.

### Carbon Credit Trading Scheme (CCTS) and GHG Emissions

- Pages: 45
- Focus: Mechanisms of the CCTS, India's emission statistics from BUR-4, and other market mechanisms like PAT and REC.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- CCTS is notified under the Energy Conservation Amendment Act (ECA), 2022.
- BEE is the Administrator and CERC is the Regulator of Carbon Trading.
- CCTS covers direct emissions (Scope 1) and indirect emissions from electricity/heat (Scope 2).
- BUR-4 states Energy is the highest emitting sector (75.66%), followed by Agriculture (13.72%).
- Carbon dioxide accounts for 80.53% of emissions, followed by Methane (13.32%).
- PAT Scheme reduces Specific Energy Consumption in energy-intensive industries via ESCerts.

#### Section Excerpt

The Carbon Credit Trading Scheme (CCTS) is notified under the Energy Conservation Amendment Act, 2022. It operates as an intensity-based baseline-and-credit system covering Scope 1 and Scope 2 emissions. The Bureau of Energy Efficiency (BEE) is the administrator, and the Central Electricity Regulatory Commission (CERC) is the regulator. India's 4th Biennial Update Report (BUR-4) notes total GHG emissions at 2,959 million tonnes of CO2 (excluding LULUCF), with the Energy sector contributing the highest (75.66%). Other market mechanisms include the PAT Scheme and Renewable Energy Certificates (REC).

#### Claims

- The Carbon Credit Trading Scheme (CCTS) is notified under the Energy Conservation Amendment Act, 2022.
- Under the Carbon Credit Trading Scheme, the Bureau of Energy Efficiency (BEE) is the administrator and the Central Electricity Regulatory Commission (CERC) is the regulator.
- The Carbon Credit Trading Scheme covers Scope 1 (direct emissions) and Scope 2 (indirect emissions from electricity/heat) emissions.
- According to India's 4th Biennial Update Report (BUR-4), the Energy sector is the highest contributor to India's GHG emissions, followed by Agriculture.
- Carbon dioxide accounts for over 80% of India's GHG emissions, followed by Methane.
- The Perform, Achieve and Trade (PAT) Scheme reduces Specific Energy Consumption in energy-intensive industries through the use of Energy Saving Certificates (ESCerts).

## Article 6 Mechanisms and Montreal Protocol

- Topic: Climate Change
- Pages: 47-48
- Rationale: Explains the market-based approaches under Article 6 of the Paris Agreement and the Kigali Amendment to the Montreal Protocol.

### Montreal Protocol and Hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs)

- Pages: 47-48
- Focus: Key provisions of the Montreal Protocol, Kigali Amendment, characteristics of HFCs, and India's achievements in phasing out Ozone Depleting Substances.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Montreal Protocol is implemented under the Vienna Convention (adopted in 1985).
- Kigali Amendment (2016) targets the phase-down of HFCs, which are non-ODS but potent greenhouse gases.
- Ozone layer recovery to 1980 values is expected by 2040 globally, 2045 over the Arctic, and 2066 over the Antarctic.
- HFCs are synthetic gases used for cooling, with an average atmospheric lifetime of 15 years, and warm the planet 3,790 times as much as CO2 over a 20-year period.
- India phased out CFCs, Carbon Tetrachloride (CTC), and halons for controlled use by 2010, ahead of Montreal Protocol schedules.

#### Section Excerpt

The Montreal Protocol (signed 1987, implemented under the 1985 Vienna Convention) is a legally binding treaty to eliminate Ozone Depleting Substances (ODS) like CFCs and Halons. The 2016 Kigali Amendment aims to phase down HFCs, which are non-ODS but potent greenhouse gases. HFCs are short-lived climate pollutants with an average atmospheric lifetime of 15 years, warming the planet 3,790 times as much as CO2 over 20 years. The ozone layer is expected to recover to 1980 values by 2040 globally, 2045 over the Arctic, and 2066 over the Antarctic. India phased out CFCs, CTC, and halons by 2010, ahead of schedule.

#### Claims

- The Montreal Protocol is a legally binding treaty implemented under the 1985 Vienna Convention to eliminate Ozone Depleting Substances.
- The 2016 Kigali Amendment targets the phase-down of Hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs), which are potent greenhouse gases but not ozone-depleting substances.
- Hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs) are short-lived climate pollutants with an average atmospheric lifetime of 15 years that have a significantly higher warming potential than CO2 over a 20-year period.
- The ozone layer is projected to recover to 1980 values by 2040 globally, with delayed recovery timelines for the Arctic (2045) and Antarctic (2066).
- India successfully phased out CFCs, Carbon Tetrachloride (CTC), and halons for controlled use by 2010, ahead of the Montreal Protocol schedules.

### Article 6 of Paris Agreement and Carbon Markets

- Pages: 47
- Focus: Mechanisms under Article 6 (6.2, 6.4, 6.8), the role of the National Designated Authority (NDA), and differences between Kyoto Protocol and Paris Agreement carbon trading.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- NDA is a 21-member committee headed by the Secretary, Environment Ministry, authorizing projects under Article 6.4.
- Article 6.2 involves decentralized bilateral trading of Internationally Transferred Mitigation Outcomes (ITMOs) with corresponding NDC adjustments.
- Article 6.4 establishes a centralized global carbon market (PACM), succeeding the Kyoto Protocol's Clean Development Mechanism (CDM).
- Article 6.8 focuses on non-market approaches with no trading of emission reductions.
- Paris Agreement allocates 5% of proceeds from Article 6.4 transactions to the Global Adaptation Fund, whereas Kyoto directed CDM proceeds to the Adaptation Fund.
- Paris Agreement restricts legacy credit use to post-2013 credits, unlike Kyoto which allowed older credits.

#### Section Excerpt

Article 6 enables countries to meet climate goals through voluntary cooperation by establishing international carbon compliance markets. Article 6.2 is a decentralized approach for bilateral cooperation trading ITMOs. Article 6.4 is a centralized Paris Agreement Crediting Mechanism (PACM) succeeding Kyoto's CDM. Article 6.8 introduces non-market approaches. The NDA, a 21-member committee headed by the Environment Ministry Secretary, recommends eligible projects like green hydrogen and CCUS. Unlike Kyoto which limited participation to developed countries, Paris includes all countries and allocates 5 percent of Article 6.4 proceeds to the Global Adaptation Fund.

#### Claims

- Article 6.2 of the Paris Agreement involves a decentralized approach for the bilateral trading of Internationally Transferred Mitigation Outcomes (ITMOs).
- Article 6.4 of the Paris Agreement establishes a centralized global carbon market known as the Paris Agreement Crediting Mechanism (PACM), which succeeds the Kyoto Protocol's Clean Development Mechanism (CDM).
- Article 6.8 of the Paris Agreement focuses exclusively on non-market approaches and does not involve the trading of emission reductions.
- The National Designated Authority (NDA) responsible for authorizing projects under Article 6.4 is a 21-member committee headed by the Secretary of the Environment Ministry.
- The Paris Agreement mandates the allocation of 5 percent of the proceeds from Article 6.4 transactions to the Global Adaptation Fund.
- The Paris Agreement restricts the use of legacy carbon credits to those generated post-2013, whereas the Kyoto Protocol allowed the use of older credits.

### Net Zero Framework for Global Shipping

- Pages: 48
- Focus: IMO's Net-Zero Framework, MARPOL Annex VI, and mandatory compliance measures for international shipping.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- The Net-Zero Framework for Global Shipping was approved by the International Maritime Organization (IMO) aiming for net-zero by around 2050.
- The framework will be included in Annex VI of MARPOL, which prevents air pollution from ships.
- Shipping accounts for almost 3% of global greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions.
- The framework becomes mandatory for large ocean-going ships over 5,000 gross tonnage.
- Ships must comply with a Global Fuel Standard to reduce their annual greenhouse gas fuel intensity (GFI).
- An IMO Net-Zero Fund will be established to collect pricing contributions from emissions.

#### Section Excerpt

The International Maritime Organization (IMO) approved the Net-Zero Framework for Global Shipping, aiming for net-zero emissions close to 2050. Shipping accounts for almost 3 percent of global GHG emissions. The Framework will be included in Annex VI of MARPOL (International Convention for the Prevention of Pollution from Ships). It is the first framework to combine mandatory emissions limits and GHG pricing across an entire sector. It applies to large ocean-going ships over 5,000 gross tonnage (emitting 85 percent of international shipping CO2). Compliance requires adhering to a Global Fuel Standard to reduce greenhouse gas fuel intensity (GFI) and a Global Economic Measure involving remedial units and financial rewards.

#### Claims

- The Net-Zero Framework for Global Shipping was approved by the International Maritime Organization (IMO) with a target of achieving net-zero emissions by around 2050.
- The Net-Zero Framework for Global Shipping will be incorporated into Annex VI of the MARPOL Convention.
- The Net-Zero Framework for Global Shipping applies mandatorily to large ocean-going ships over 5,000 gross tonnage.
- The Net-Zero Framework for Global Shipping is the first framework to combine mandatory emissions limits and greenhouse gas pricing across an entire sector.
- Compliance with the Net-Zero Framework for Global Shipping requires adherence to a Global Fuel Standard to reduce greenhouse gas fuel intensity (GFI).

## IMO Conventions and Carbon Capture Technologies

- Topic: Climate Change
- Pages: 49-50
- Rationale: Details maritime pollution conventions (MARPOL) and the R&D roadmap for Carbon Capture, Utilization, and Storage (CCUS).

### Carbon Capture and Utilisation (CCU) and Ocean-based CCUS

- Pages: 49-50
- Focus: Technologies for capturing CO2 (DAC, industrial) and utilizing it (EOR, fuels, chemicals), plus ocean-based storage methods.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- The Department of Science and Technology (DST) launched India's first R&D Roadmap for Net Zero through CCUS.
- Direct Air Capture (DAC) isolates CO2 directly from ambient air using a gas trapping system.
- Direct Utilisation of CO2 includes Enhanced Oil Recovery (EOR) and food/healthcare applications.
- CO2-to-Products includes conversion to methanol/fuels via Fischer-Tropsch synthesis and mineral carbonates for building materials.
- Ocean Alkalinity Enhancement (OAE) accelerates CO2 absorption by adding pulverized minerals like lime.
- Ocean fertilisation promotes phytoplankton growth by adding micronutrients like phosphorus and nitrogen.

#### Section Excerpt

Carbon Capture and Utilisation (CCU) involves capturing CO2 from industrial sources or via Direct Air Capture (DAC) and using it as feedstock. Direct utilization includes Enhanced Oil Recovery (EOR) and industrial uses, while CO2-to-Products involves converting CO2 into chemicals (urea, plastics), fuels (methanol via Fischer-Tropsch), and mineral carbonates (cement). Ocean-based CCUS includes Ocean Alkalinity Enhancement (OAE) and Ocean Fertilisation to boost CO2 absorption.

#### Claims

- The Department of Science and Technology (DST) launched India's first R&D Roadmap for Net Zero through CCUS.
- Direct Air Capture (DAC) isolates CO2 directly from ambient air rather than from industrial point sources.
- Direct Utilisation of captured CO2 includes its use in Enhanced Oil Recovery (EOR).
- Captured CO2 can be converted into fuels such as methanol via Fischer-Tropsch synthesis.
- Ocean Alkalinity Enhancement (OAE) accelerates CO2 absorption by adding pulverized minerals like lime to the ocean.
- Ocean fertilisation promotes phytoplankton growth to boost CO2 absorption by adding micronutrients like phosphorus and nitrogen.

### International Maritime Organization (IMO) and Key Conventions

- Pages: 49
- Focus: IMO's structure, membership, and major maritime conventions like SOLAS, MARPOL, and the Hong Kong Convention.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- IMO is a UN specialized agency for shipping safety and marine/atmospheric pollution prevention.
- India is one of the 176 Member States of the IMO.
- The Maritime Safety Committee (MSC) is the IMO's highest technical body.
- Key conventions include SOLAS (1974), MARPOL, and the Hong Kong Convention (2009) for ship recycling.

#### Section Excerpt

The International Maritime Organization (IMO) is a UN specialized agency responsible for shipping safety and preventing marine pollution. Established via a 1948 Geneva convention (in force 1958), it has 176 members, including India. Its highest technical body is the Maritime Safety Committee (MSC). Key conventions include SOLAS (1974), MARPOL, and the Hong Kong Convention (2009) for safe and environmentally sound ship recycling.

#### Claims

- The International Maritime Organization (IMO) is a specialized agency of the United Nations responsible for shipping safety and preventing marine pollution.
- India is a member state of the International Maritime Organization (IMO).
- The Maritime Safety Committee (MSC) is the highest technical body of the International Maritime Organization (IMO).
- The Hong Kong Convention (2009) is an IMO convention that regulates the safe and environmentally sound recycling of ships.

### Glacier Preservation and Glacial Loss

- Pages: 50
- Focus: Recent declarations of dead glaciers and the environmental consequences of glacial melting.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Nepal's Yala Glacier was declared dead after shrinking 66% since the 1970s.
- Okjokull glacier in Iceland had the world's first glacier funeral in 2019.
- Glacial loss reduces the albedo effect, leading to increased heat absorption.
- Melting glaciers have contributed nearly 2 cm to global sea level rise since 2001.
- Glaciers store nearly three-quarters of Earth's freshwater.

#### Section Excerpt

Nepal's Yala Glacier was recently declared dead after shrinking 66% since the 1970s. Other dead glaciers include Pizol (Switzerland), Clark (US), Ayoloco (Mexico), and Okjokull (Iceland), which had the world's first glacier funeral in 2019. Consequences of glacial loss include reduced albedo effect (increasing heat absorption), a 2 cm global sea level rise since 2001, loss of freshwater (glaciers store nearly 75% of Earth's freshwater), and increased risks of Glacial Lake Outburst Floods (GLOFs) and avalanches.

#### Claims

- Okjokull is a glacier located in Iceland that had the world's first glacier funeral in 2019.
- Glacial loss leads to a reduced albedo effect, which consequently increases heat absorption by the Earth.
- Glaciers are the largest reservoir of freshwater on Earth, storing nearly 75 percent of it.
- The Yala Glacier, which was recently declared dead, is located in Nepal.

### Methane Emissions and the Climate and Clean Air Coalition (CCAC)

- Pages: 50
- Focus: Methane's properties as a GHG, major emission sources, and the role of the CCAC.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- The Global Methane Status Report 2025 was released by UNEP and the CCAC.
- CCAC (founded 2012, convened within UNEP) targets short-lived climate pollutants: methane, black carbon, HFCs, and tropospheric ozone.
- India joined the Climate and Clean Air Coalition (CCAC) in 2019.
- Methane is over 80 times more potent than CO2 over a 20-year period but persists for only 7-12 years.
- 60% of methane emissions are anthropogenic; wetlands are the largest natural source.
- India is the world's 3rd largest methane emitter, primarily driven by stubble burning.

#### Section Excerpt

The Global Methane Status Report 2025 was released by UNEP and the Climate and Clean Air Coalition (CCAC). The CCAC, founded in 2012 within UNEP, targets short-lived climate pollutants (methane, black carbon, HFCs, tropospheric ozone); India joined in 2019. Methane is the second biggest driver of climate change, over 80 times more potent than CO2 over 20 years, but persists for only 7-12 years. 60% of emissions are human-driven (agriculture, fossil fuels, landfills), while wetlands are the largest natural source. India is the 3rd largest emitter.

#### Claims

- The Global Methane Status Report 2025 is jointly released by the United Nations Environment Programme (UNEP) and the Climate and Clean Air Coalition (CCAC).
- The Climate and Clean Air Coalition (CCAC) specifically targets short-lived climate pollutants, including methane, black carbon, hydrofluorocarbons (HFCs), and tropospheric ozone.
- India is a member of the Climate and Clean Air Coalition (CCAC), having joined the initiative in 2019.
- Methane is over 80 times more potent than carbon dioxide over a 20-year period but has a much shorter atmospheric lifespan of only 7 to 12 years.
- While 60 percent of methane emissions are anthropogenic, wetlands constitute the largest natural source of methane emissions.
- India is the world's third-largest emitter of methane, with its emissions primarily driven by agricultural activities such as stubble burning.

### India's Polar Research Stations and Maitri II

- Maitri II is India's upcoming 4th research base in Eastern Antarctica, expected to be operational by 2029.

- India's Antarctic stations include Dakshin Gangotri (decommissioned), Maitri, and Bharati.
- Himadri is India's active research station in the Arctic.

Union Finance Ministry granted approval to Maitri II, new research station in Eastern Antarctica. This will be India’s 4th research base, expected to be operational by January 2029. It would be established as a green research base powered using renewable energy sources... India’s other Polar research stations: Maitri (Antarctica), Bharati (Antarctica), Dakshin Gangotri (first scientific base station of India situated in Antarctica now decommissioned), and Himadri (Arctic).

- Maitri II is India's upcoming fourth research base located in Eastern Antarctica, expected to be operational by 2029.
- Maitri II will be established as a green research base powered by renewable energy sources such as solar and wind energy.
- Dakshin Gangotri was India's first scientific base station in Antarctica and is currently decommissioned.
- Himadri is India's active polar research station located in the Arctic region.
- The Indian Antarctic Act was enacted in 2022 to provide a domestic legal framework for India's polar activities.

### Arctic Amplification

- Pages: 52
- Focus: Causes and impacts of the Arctic warming faster than the rest of the globe.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Arctic Amplification is the phenomenon where the Arctic warms significantly faster than the rest of the Earth.
- Reduced albedo from melting reflective ice acts as a feedback loop driving Arctic Amplification.
- Lapse rate feedback in the Arctic causes greenhouse gas warming to be most pronounced near the surface, unlike in the tropics.
- The Water Vapour Triple Effect contributes to amplification by creating more cloud cover and acting as a greenhouse gas.
- Arctic Amplification impacts India by disrupting the Indian Monsoon and contributing to sea level rise.

#### Section Excerpt

About Arctic Amplification: A phenomenon where climate in Arctic warms more quickly than the rest of the Earth. Factors for Arctic Amplification: Reduced Albedo due to melting of reflective ice... Lapse rate feedback (warming from GHG most pronounced near the surface...); Water Vapour Triple Effect... Atmospheric Heat transport from tropics. Impacts on India: Disruption of Indian Monsoon; Sea level rise etc.

#### Claims

- Arctic Amplification is the phenomenon where the Arctic region warms significantly faster than the rest of the Earth.
- Reduced albedo due to the melting of reflective ice acts as a feedback loop that drives Arctic Amplification.
- Lapse rate feedback in the Arctic causes greenhouse gas warming to be most pronounced near the surface, unlike in the tropics.
- The Water Vapour Triple Effect contributes to Arctic Amplification by creating more cloud cover and acting as a greenhouse gas.
- Arctic Amplification impacts India by disrupting the Indian Monsoon and contributing to sea level rise.

### Climate Monitoring Projects: AmazonFACE and NASA OCO

- Pages: 52
- Focus: Scientific projects monitoring CO2 absorption, forest resilience, and atmospheric carbon.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 3

#### Evidence Bullets

- AmazonFACE is a Brazilian project simulating future atmospheres to assess how tropical forests adapt to rising CO2 and temperatures.
- NASA's Orbiting Carbon Observatory (OCO) missions are key data sources for tracking atmospheric carbon.
- OCO findings reveal that the Amazon rainforest currently emits more CO2 than it absorbs.
- Boreal forests and melting permafrost areas in Canada and Russia currently absorb more CO2 than they emit.

#### Section Excerpt

AmazonFACE: Scientists in Brazil have launched the AmazonFACE (Free-Air CO2 Enrichment) project near Manaus. It seeks to simulate the atmosphere of the future and assess how tropical forests adapt to rising CO2... NASA’ Orbiting Carbon Observatory (OCO): The USA administration plans to shut down NASA’s OCO missions... Key Findings: Amazon rainforest emits more CO2 than it absorbs. Boreal forests in Canada, Russia, and melting permafrost areas absorb more CO2 than they emit.

#### Claims

- AmazonFACE is a Brazilian project that uses Free-Air CO2 Enrichment to simulate future atmospheric conditions and assess tropical forest adaptation to rising CO2.
- Data from NASA's Orbiting Carbon Observatory (OCO) reveals that the Amazon rainforest currently emits more CO2 than it absorbs, acting as a net carbon source.
- Boreal forests and melting permafrost areas in Canada and Russia currently absorb more CO2 than they emit, acting as net carbon sinks.

### Climate Tipping Points

- Pages: 52
- Focus: Definition, mechanisms, and examples of climate tipping points as identified by the IPCC.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Climate tipping points are critical thresholds that lead to significant, often irreversible, system changes when exceeded.
- Tipping points are interlinked, meaning crossing one can create cascading effects that threaten planetary stability.
- IPCC-identified tipping points include Amazon dieback, permafrost collapse, AMOC slowdown, and ice sheet collapse.
- Warm-water coral reefs have recently crossed their thermal tipping point, triggering irreversible dieback.

#### Section Excerpt

About climate tipping point: These are critical thresholds in a system that, when exceeded, can lead to a significant change in the state of the system, often with an understanding that the change is irreversible... Tipping points are interlinked, meaning crossing one may accelerate collapse of others... Tipping points identified by IPCC: Amazon rainforest dieback, permafrost collapse, Atlantic Meridional Overturning Circulation (AMOC) slowdown, Boreal Forest shift, Greenland and Antarctic ice sheets collapse risks.

#### Claims

- Climate tipping points are critical thresholds that, when exceeded, lead to significant and often irreversible changes in the state of the system.
- Climate tipping points are interlinked, meaning that crossing one threshold can create cascading effects that accelerate the collapse of others.
- The IPCC has identified Amazon rainforest dieback, permafrost collapse, Atlantic Meridional Overturning Circulation (AMOC) slowdown, Boreal Forest shift, and ice sheet collapse as climate tipping points.
- Warm-water coral reefs have recently crossed their thermal tipping point, which has triggered their irreversible dieback.

### United Nations Environment Programme (UNEP)

- Pages: 54
- Focus: UNEP's genesis, funding, hosted entities (IPCC, IPBES), administered multilateral agreements, and key reports.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- Established in 1972 following the UN Conference on Human Environment, headquartered in Nairobi, Kenya.
- Relies on voluntary contributions for over 95% of funding; Environment Fund is its core fund.
- Hosts secretariats for IPBES and IPCC (jointly with WMO).
- Administers major agreements including CITES, CMS, Vienna Convention, Basel, CBD, Rotterdam, Stockholm, and Minamata Conventions.
- Publishes key reports: Emission Gap Report, Adaptation Gap Report, Global Environment Outlook, and State of Finance for Forests.
- Supported the Common Carbon Metric for assessing building operations' carbon footprint.

#### Section Excerpt

Location: Nairobi, Kenya. Genesis: 1972, following UN Conference on Human Environment... Hosts the secretariats of various conventions and Entities: IPBES, IPCC... Major Global Multilateral Agreements under UNEP: CITES, CMS, Vienna, Basel, CBD, Rotterdam, Stockholm, Minamata... Reports: Emission Gap, Adaptation Gap, Global Environment Outlook 7.

#### Claims

- The United Nations Environment Programme (UNEP) was established in 1972 following the UN Conference on Human Environment and is headquartered in Nairobi, Kenya.
- Over 95% of UNEP's funding comes from voluntary contributions, with the Environment Fund serving as its core fund.
- UNEP hosts the secretariat for IPBES and jointly hosts the IPCC secretariat with the World Meteorological Organization (WMO).
- UNEP administers several major multilateral environmental agreements, including CITES, CMS, CBD, and the Basel, Rotterdam, Stockholm, and Minamata Conventions.
- UNEP publishes the Emission Gap Report, Adaptation Gap Report, Global Environment Outlook, and State of Finance for Forests.
- The Common Carbon Metric, supported by UNEP, is used for assessing the carbon footprint of building operations.

## BRS Conventions and Minamata Convention

- Topic: Pollution Conventions
- Pages: 58-59
- Rationale: Covers the latest COP outcomes for Basel, Rotterdam, and Stockholm conventions, and the Minamata Convention on Mercury.

### Basel, Rotterdam, and Stockholm (BRS) Conventions

- Pages: 58
- Focus: Recent COP outcomes, specific chemical additions, and core mandates of the BRS conventions.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Basel Convention (1989) enforces Prior Informed Consent (PIC) for transboundary hazardous wastes, excludes radioactive waste, and India is a party.
- Rotterdam Convention (2004) creates a legally binding PIC procedure for international trade of certain hazardous chemicals/pesticides but does not outright ban them.
- Stockholm Convention (2004) targets Persistent Organic Pollutants (POPs); India ratified it in 2006.
- Rotterdam COP-12 added Carbosulfan and Fenthion to Annex III.
- Stockholm COP-12 added Chlorpyrifos, LC-PFCAs, and MCCPs to Annex A, and amended exemptions for UV-328 (a phenolic benzotriazole used as a UV stabilizer).

#### Section Excerpt

The 17th Basel, 12th Rotterdam, and 12th Stockholm Convention meetings in Geneva yielded key updates. Basel (hazardous wastes) launched work on textile waste and updated POPs guidelines. Rotterdam (PIC for hazardous chemicals) added Carbosulfan and Fenthion to Annex III. Stockholm (POPs) added Chlorpyrifos, LC-PFCAs, and MCCPs to Annex A, and amended UV-328 exemptions. India is a party to all three. Basel enforces Prior Informed Consent (PIC) for transboundary waste but excludes radioactive waste. Rotterdam promotes informed decisions on international trade of banned/restricted chemicals without outright banning them. Stockholm aims to protect health and environment from persistent organic pollutants.

#### Claims

- The Basel Convention enforces Prior Informed Consent (PIC) for transboundary hazardous wastes but explicitly excludes radioactive waste.
- The Rotterdam Convention establishes a legally binding Prior Informed Consent (PIC) procedure for the international trade of certain hazardous chemicals, but it does not outright ban their trade.
- India is a party to all three BRS Conventions: Basel, Rotterdam, and Stockholm.
- Carbosulfan and Fenthion were added to Annex III of the Rotterdam Convention during COP-12.
- Chlorpyrifos, LC-PFCAs, and MCCPs were added to Annex A of the Stockholm Convention during COP-12.

### Global Plastic Pollution Treaty

- Pages: 59
- Focus: INC negotiations, country groupings (Like Minded vs. High Ambition), and the historical evolution of plastic pollution treaties.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- The Global Plastic Pollution Treaty is being negotiated by the INC under a 2022 UNEA resolution to create a legally binding instrument addressing the plastic lifecycle.
- Like Minded Countries, including India, China, and Saudi Arabia, reject restrictions on plastic production.
- The High Ambition Coalition, including the European Union, advocates for essential production caps on plastics.
- MARPOL Annex V (1988) was the first treaty banning plastics in ship-based discharges.
- The 2019 Basel Convention Amendments brought plastic waste under global notification and Prior Informed Consent (PIC) rules.

#### Section Excerpt

Intergovernmental Negotiating Committee (INC) talks for a Global Plastic Pollution Treaty, mandated by a 2022 UNEA resolution, recently adjourned. The treaty aims for an international legally binding instrument addressing the full plastic lifecycle. Disagreements persist: Like Minded Countries (including India, China, Saudi Arabia) reject restrictions on plastic production, whereas the High Ambition Coalition (around 100 countries, including the EU) argues production caps are essential. The evolution of global plastic regulation includes MARPOL Annex V (1988) banning ship-based plastic discharges, the 2019 Basel Convention amendments bringing plastic waste under PIC rules, and SDG 12 & 14.1.

#### Claims

- The Global Plastic Pollution Treaty is being negotiated as an international legally binding instrument that addresses the full lifecycle of plastics.
- India is part of the 'Like Minded Countries' group that rejects restrictions on plastic production in the Global Plastic Pollution Treaty negotiations.
- MARPOL Annex V is an international regulation that bans the discharge of plastics from ships into the marine environment.
- The 2019 amendments to the Basel Convention mandate that the transboundary movement of plastic waste is subject to Prior Informed Consent (PIC) rules.

### Minamata Convention and Mercury Pollution

- Pages: 59
- Focus: COP-6 outcomes, Minamata Convention provisions, and characteristics/toxicity of Mercury.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- The Minamata Convention (effective 2017) is a legally binding treaty to protect human health and the environment from mercury; India is a member.
- COP-6 agreed to phase out mercury-based dental amalgams by 2034 and eliminate mercury-added skin-lightening products.
- Mercury is the only common metal that is liquid at room temperature; anthropogenic sources include coal-based thermal power plants and gold mining.
- Mercury converts into highly toxic methylmercury, leading to bioaccumulation in fish and animals.
- There is no known safe level of mercury exposure for humans.

#### Section Excerpt

COP-6 to the Minamata Convention on Mercury agreed to phase out mercury-based dental amalgams by 2034, eliminate mercury-added skin-lightening products, and strengthen action on artisanal and small-scale gold mining (ASGM). The Minamata Convention is a legally binding treaty (effective 2017) with UNEP secretariat support; India is a member. Mercury is a naturally occurring heavy metal, liquid at room temperature, sourced from coal ash, thermal plants, and gold mining. Emitted mercury converts into highly toxic methylmercury, which bioaccumulates in aquatic food chains. There is no known safe level of mercury exposure for humans.

#### Claims

- The Minamata Convention is a legally binding treaty effective from 2017, and India is a member state.
- COP-6 to the Minamata Convention set a target to phase out mercury-based dental amalgams by 2034.
- Mercury is a naturally occurring heavy metal and the only common metal that is liquid at room temperature.
- Coal-based thermal power plants and gold mining are major anthropogenic sources of mercury emissions.
- Mercury emitted into the environment converts into highly toxic methylmercury, which bioaccumulates in aquatic food chains.
- There is no known safe level of mercury exposure for humans.

## Microplastics and Urban Air Pollution

- Topic: Pollution
- Pages: 60-61
- Rationale: Details the types and impacts of microplastics and the Graded Response Action Plan (GRAP) for NCR air pollution.

### Plastic-Related Chemical Terms and Bioremediation

- Pages: 60-61
- Focus: Specific polymers, plasticizers (Phthalates), and plastic-degrading bacteria in the news.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Ideonella sakaiensis is a plastic-eating bacteria capable of degrading PET (Polyethylene Terephthalate).
- PBAT is a biodegradable thermoplastic copolymer derived from renewable resources like corn starch and sugarcane.
- Phthalates are plasticizers added to plastics like PVC to make them soft and less brittle, but they do not permanently bind to the plastic surfaces.
- Phthalates are categorized as hazardous endocrine-disrupting chemicals (EDCs) that negatively affect reproductive health.

#### Section Excerpt

Bacteria Ideonella sakaiensis: Plastic eating bacteria that can degrade PET (Polyethylene Terephthalate)... Polybutylene Adipate Terephthalate (PBAT): Biodegradable, chemical-free thermoplastic copolymer made from renewable resources, like corn starch and sugarcane... Pthalates: Ortho-phthalates, or “phthalates,” are chemicals used in plastic products (commonly in specific type of plastic named polyvinyl chloride) to make the material soft and less brittle... Categorized as hazardous endocrine-disrupting chemicals (EDCs); affect reproductive health.

#### Claims

- Ideonella sakaiensis is a species of bacteria capable of degrading Polyethylene Terephthalate (PET) plastic.
- Polybutylene Adipate Terephthalate (PBAT) is a biodegradable thermoplastic copolymer derived from renewable resources like corn starch and sugarcane.
- Phthalates are chemical plasticizers added to plastics like polyvinyl chloride (PVC) to make them softer and less brittle.
- Phthalates are categorized as hazardous endocrine-disrupting chemicals (EDCs) that negatively affect reproductive health.

### Plastic and Microplastic Pollution

- Pages: 60
- Focus: Characteristics, types, and impacts of microplastics, along with India's regulatory framework (PWM Rules).
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Microplastics are up to 5 millimetres in diameter, while nanoplastics are less than 1,000 nanometers.
- Primary microplastics are intentionally manufactured (e.g., microbeads in cosmetics), whereas secondary microplastics result from the breakdown of larger items (e.g., microfibers).
- Microplastics reduce plants' photosynthetic ability by around 12% and cause biomagnification in marine life.
- The Plastic Waste Management (Amendment) Rules, 2021 raised the thickness limit of plastic carry bags to 120 microns effective December 31, 2022.

#### Section Excerpt

Types: Microplastic (up to 5 millimetres in diameter); Nanoplastics (less than 1,000 nanometers)... Primary Microplastics: Intentionally manufactured. E.g. Plastic pellets, Microbeads... Secondary Microplastics: From breakdown of larger plastic items. E.g. Microfibers... PWM (Amendment) Rules, 2021: Raised thickness limit (50 microns to 75 microns starting September 30, 2021, and then to 120 microns from December 31, 2022) for carry bags.

#### Claims

- Microplastics are defined as plastic particles up to 5 millimetres in diameter, whereas nanoplastics are smaller than 1,000 nanometers.
- Primary microplastics are intentionally manufactured, such as microbeads, while secondary microplastics are formed from the breakdown of larger plastic items, such as microfibers.
- Microplastics cause biomagnification in marine life and reduce the photosynthetic ability of plants by approximately 12 percent.
- The Plastic Waste Management (Amendment) Rules, 2021, increased the thickness limit for plastic carry bags to 120 microns, effective from December 31, 2022.

### Urban Air Pollution and GRAP

- Pages: 61
- Focus: GRAP stages, IQAir report findings, WHO vs NAAQS standards, and India's mitigation initiatives.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- GRAP is an emergency response mechanism implemented by CAQM, originally created on Supreme Court directions.
- GRAP Stage 4 (Severe + category) is triggered when the AQI exceeds 451.
- The street-canyon effect, caused by narrow streets with tall buildings, traps pollutants and exacerbates urban air pollution.
- India's NAAQS limit for PM2.5 is 40 µg/m³, which is significantly higher than the WHO 2021 guideline of 5 µg/m³.
- The SATAT initiative aims to create a Compressed Bio-Gas (CBG) ecosystem to help curb air pollution.

#### Section Excerpt

Graded Response Action Plan (GRAP)... Emergency response mechanism based on AQI level of Delhi implemented by CAQM... Stage 1: Poor category (AQI 201 to 300)... Stage 4: Severe + category (AQI 451+)... Causes: Winter weather conditions like temperature inversion; Narrow streets with tall buildings (street-canyon effect) trapping pollutants... Comparison of Air Quality Standards: PM2.5 WHO 2021 Guideline is 5 µg/m³, India (NAAQS) is 40 µg/m³... Steps taken to curb Air Pollution: Promotion of electric mobility under PM E-DRIVE Scheme... SATAT initiative for creating Compressed Bio-Gas (CBG) ecosystem.

#### Claims

- The Graded Response Action Plan (GRAP) is an emergency response mechanism implemented by the Commission for Air Quality Management (CAQM).
- GRAP Stage 4, classified as the 'Severe +' category, is triggered when the Air Quality Index (AQI) exceeds 451.
- The street-canyon effect occurs when narrow streets with tall buildings trap pollutants, thereby exacerbating urban air pollution.
- India's National Ambient Air Quality Standards (NAAQS) limit for PM2.5 is 40 µg/m³, which is significantly higher than the WHO 2021 guideline of 5 µg/m³.
- The SATAT initiative aims to curb air pollution by creating a Compressed Bio-Gas (CBG) ecosystem.

## Cloud Seeding, AQI, and Aerosols
