### Swachh Vayu Sarveshan Award 2025 & NCAP

- Pages: 62-63
- Focus: Award categories, winning cities, and National Clean Air Programme (NCAP) targets.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Indore ranked 1st in Category 1 (Population >10 lakh) of the Swachh Vayu Sarveshan Award 2025.
- NCAP targets up to a 40% reduction in PM10 levels by 2025-26 from 2019-20 levels.
- NCAP covers 131 non-attainment and Million Plus Cities across 24 States/UTs.
- The PRANA portal monitors the implementation of NCAP.

#### Section Excerpt

Awards announced by MoEFCC recognizing best-performing cities among 130 NCAP cities. Category 1 (>10 lakh) won by Indore; Category 2 (3-10 lakh) by Amravati; Category 3 (<3 lakh) by Dewas. NCAP (launched 2019) targets a reduction in PM10 level up to 40% or achievement of national ambient air quality standards (60 microgram/m3) by 2025-26 from 2019-20 levels. Implemented by CPCB and monitored via the PRANA portal.

#### Claims

- The Swachh Vayu Sarveshan Award is announced by the MoEFCC, with Indore ranking 1st in Category 1 (population >10 lakh) for 2025.
- The National Clean Air Programme (NCAP) targets up to a 40% reduction in PM10 levels or achieving national ambient air quality standards by 2025-26 from 2019-20 levels.
- NCAP covers 131 non-attainment and Million Plus Cities across 24 States/UTs.
- NCAP is implemented by the Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) and its implementation is monitored via the PRANA portal.

### Cloud Seeding

- Pages: 62
- Focus: Scientific weather modification technique and specific seeding agents.
- Question quota hint: 1
- Claim count: 3

#### Evidence Bullets

- Cloud seeding is conducted on existing natural clouds and does not create new clouds.
- Silver Iodide (AgI) is the most common seeding agent due to its efficient ice nucleating properties.
- Other chemicals used include potassium iodide, sulfur dioxide, and dry ice.

#### Section Excerpt

Scientific weather modification technique involving dispersing agents like silver iodide into moisture-bearing clouds to induce precipitation. Conducted on existing natural clouds and does not create clouds. Seeding Agents: Silver Iodide (AgI), most common material, known for its efficient ice nucleating properties. Other chemicals used are potassium iodide (KI), sulfur dioxide (SO2), frozen carbon dioxide (dry ice).

#### Claims

- Cloud seeding is a weather modification technique that can only be conducted on existing natural clouds and cannot create new clouds.
- Silver Iodide (AgI) is the most common cloud seeding agent due to its efficient ice nucleating properties.
- Chemicals such as potassium iodide, sulfur dioxide, and frozen carbon dioxide (dry ice) are utilized as agents in cloud seeding.

### Commission for Air Quality Management in NCR and Adjoining Areas (CAQM)

- Pages: 62
- Focus: Statutory backing, nodal ministry, jurisdiction, and overriding powers of the CAQM.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- CAQM is a statutory body under the Ministry of Environment, Forest and Climate Change.
- Its jurisdiction covers the NCR and adjoining areas in Haryana, Punjab, Rajasthan, and Uttar Pradesh.
- CAQM orders override those of state governments, CPCB, and state PCBs in case of conflict.
- The Chairperson requires at least 15 years of experience in environment protection or 25 years in administration.

#### Section Excerpt

Statutory body under Commission for Air Quality Management in National Capital Region and Adjoining Areas Act 2021. Nodal Ministry: Ministry of Environment, Forest and Climate Change. Protects and improves air quality in NCR and Adjoining Areas (Haryana, Punjab, Rajasthan and Uttar Pradesh). Its orders override those of state governments, CPCB, state PCBs, and others in case of conflict. Chairperson must have at least 15 years in environment protection or 25 years in administration.

#### Claims

- The Commission for Air Quality Management (CAQM) is a statutory body functioning under the Ministry of Environment, Forest and Climate Change.
- The jurisdiction of CAQM covers the National Capital Region (NCR) and adjoining areas in the states of Haryana, Punjab, Rajasthan, and Uttar Pradesh.
- In case of a conflict, the orders of the CAQM override those of state governments, the Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB), and state Pollution Control Boards (PCBs).
- The Chairperson of the CAQM must possess at least 15 years of experience in environment protection or 25 years of experience in administration.

### National Air Quality Index (AQI)

- Pages: 62

- Claim count: 2

- The index monitors exactly 8 pollutants: PM10, PM2.5, NO2, SO2, CO, O3, NH3, and Pb.

#### Section Excerpt

AQI categorizes air quality into six ranges: Good (0-50), Satisfactory (51-100), Moderate (101-200), Poor (201-300), Very Poor (300-400), and Severe (>400). Monitored Pollutants: PM10, PM2.5, NO2, SO2, CO, O3, NH3, Pb.

#### Claims

- The National Air Quality Index (AQI) classifies air quality into exactly six categories ranging from Good to Severe.
- The National Air Quality Index (AQI) monitors exactly eight pollutants: PM10, PM2.5, NO2, SO2, CO, O3, NH3, and Pb.

### Aerosols and Urban Wind Stilling Effect

- Pages: 63
- Focus: Types of aerosols, their climate impacts (Twomey Effect), and the creation of Urban Aerosol Clean Islands.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Secondary aerosols are formed from gases through chemical reactions, such as sulfates from industrial emissions.
- The Twomey Effect describes how increased anthropogenic aerosols make clouds brighter by creating more tiny droplets that reflect more sunlight.
- Aerosols act as condensation nuclei, causing cloud droplets to be smaller and longer-lived.
- The Urban Wind Stilling Effect creates Urban Aerosol Clean Islands by acting as an invisible barrier that slows long-range dust and pollutant entry into cities.

#### Section Excerpt

Aerosols are tiny solid or liquid particles suspended in the atmosphere. Primary aerosols are directly emitted (sea spray, dust); secondary are formed from gases through chemical reactions (sulfates). They act as condensation nuclei, causing cloud droplets to be smaller and longer-lived, leading to solar dimming. The Twomey Effect describes how increased anthropogenic aerosols make clouds brighter by creating more tiny droplets. Urban Wind Stilling Effect creates Urban Aerosol Clean Islands where weakened surface winds due to urban structures block long-range dust and pollutant entry, resulting in lower aerosol levels than nearby rural areas.

#### Claims

- Secondary aerosols are formed from gases through chemical reactions, whereas primary aerosols are directly emitted into the atmosphere.
- Aerosols act as condensation nuclei, causing cloud droplets to be smaller and longer-lived, which leads to solar dimming.
- The Twomey Effect describes the phenomenon where increased anthropogenic aerosols make clouds brighter by creating more tiny droplets that reflect more sunlight.
- The Urban Wind Stilling Effect creates Urban Aerosol Clean Islands by weakening surface winds, which blocks long-range dust and pollutants and results in lower aerosol levels than nearby rural areas.

Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) released assessment report on Polluted River Stretches for Restoration of Water Quality - 2025. CPCB executes National Water Quality Monitoring Programme (NWMP) with State Pollution Control Boards/Pollution Control Committees and has been identifying the country's Polluted River Stretches since 2009.

#### Claims

- The National Water Quality Monitoring Programme (NWMP) is executed by the Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) in collaboration with State Pollution Control Boards and Committees.
- The Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) has been identifying the polluted river stretches in India since the year 2009.

### Framework and Programmes for River Rejuvenation

- Pages: 64-65
- Focus: Legal frameworks and major national programmes (Namami Gange, NMCG, NGRBA, NRCP) for river conservation.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- The Namami Gange Programme is implemented by the National Mission for Clean Ganga (NMCG), using the river basin as the unit of planning.
- NMCG was registered as a society in 2011 under the Societies Registration Act, 1860.
- NMCG acts as the implementation arm of the National Ganga River Basin Authority (NGRBA).
- NGRBA was constituted under the Environment Protection Act, 1986, replacing the National Ganga Council in 2016.
- The National River Conservation Plan (NRCP) is a Centrally Sponsored Scheme covering identified river stretches excluding the Ganga basin.

#### Section Excerpt

Legal Framework: Water Act, 1974... Environment Protection Act, 1986... River Rejuvenation Programmes: Namami Gange Programme: Superseded Ganga Action Plan (GAP) launched in 1985 implemented by National Mission for Clean Ganga (NMCG) with River basin is the unit of planning and management. NMCG: Registered as a society in 2011 under Societies Registration Act, 1860, acts as the implementation arm of National Ganga River Basin Authority (NGRBA)... NGRBA: Constituted under EPA, 1986 replacing National Ganga Council (chaired by PM) in 2016... Centrally Sponsored Scheme of National River Conservation Plan (NRCP): In identified stretches of rivers, excluding in Ganga basin.

#### Claims

- The Namami Gange Programme is implemented by the National Mission for Clean Ganga (NMCG) and uses the river basin as its unit of planning.
- The National Mission for Clean Ganga (NMCG) was registered as a society under the Societies Registration Act, 1860.
- The National Ganga River Basin Authority (NGRBA) was constituted under the Environment Protection Act, 1986.
- The National River Conservation Plan (NRCP) is a Centrally Sponsored Scheme that covers identified river stretches but excludes the Ganga basin.

### Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) and Environmental Compensation

- Pages: 64
- Focus: Statutory origins, functions, and powers of the CPCB and SPCBs, particularly regarding Environmental Compensation.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 3

#### Evidence Bullets

- CPCB is a statutory body established under the Water (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act, 1974.
- CPCB is also entrusted with powers and functions under the Air (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act, 1981.
- CPCB provides technical services to the Environment Ministry under the Environment (Protection) Act, 1986.
- State Pollution Control Boards (SPCBs) are constituted under the same acts to implement environmental laws within state jurisdictions.
- Per a Supreme Court ruling, PCBs can impose Environmental Compensation (EC) under the Water Act and Air Act for environmental damage, which is distinct from punitive action.

#### Section Excerpt

Genesis: Statutory body under Water (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act, 1974. Entrusted with powers and functions under Air (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act, 1981. Ministry: Ministry of Environment, Forest & Climate Change... Power to impose Environmental Compensation (EC): As per Supreme Court ruling, PCBs can impose or collect EC under Water Act, 1974 and Air Act, 1981 in case of environmental damage by erring entity or when damage is imminent, not for every violation.

#### Claims

- The Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) is a statutory body established under the Water (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act, 1974.
- In addition to the Water Act, the CPCB is entrusted with powers under the Air (Prevention and Control of Pollution) Act, 1981, and provides technical services under the Environment (Protection) Act, 1986.
- Pollution Control Boards (PCBs) can impose Environmental Compensation (EC) under the Water Act, 1974 and Air Act, 1981 specifically for environmental damage or imminent damage, rather than for every violation.

### Polluted River Stretches (PRS) and Water Quality Criteria

- Pages: 64
- Focus: Definition of PRS, Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD) criteria, and geographical distribution of polluted stretches in India.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- PRS is identified when two or more polluted locations occur in a continuous sequence on a river.
- The primary criterion for identifying a Polluted River Stretch is a Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD) exceeding 3 mg/L.
- PRS are categorized into Five Priority Classes (I to V) based on the maximum BOD observed.
- The total number of PRS in India has decreased from 351 in 2018 to 296 in 2025.
- Maharashtra has the highest number of Polluted River Stretches (54) in India.

#### Section Excerpt

Two or more polluted locations on a river in a continuous sequence are considered as stretch and identified as PRS. Criteria: Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD) exceeds 3 milligrams/litre (mg/L). BOD is an indicator of water quality, measuring oxygen needed to break down organic matter. Categories: Five Priority Classes (I to V) based on maximum BOD observed... Total number of PRS has decreased from 351 (in 2018) to 296 (in 2025). Geographic Distribution: Maharashtra has highest number (54) of PRS.

#### Claims

- A river stretch is identified as a Polluted River Stretch (PRS) if its Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD) exceeds 3 milligrams/litre (mg/L).
- Polluted River Stretches are categorized into five priority classes (I to V) based on the maximum Biochemical Oxygen Demand (BOD) observed.
- The total number of Polluted River Stretches (PRS) in India has decreased between 2018 and 2025.
- Maharashtra has the highest number of Polluted River Stretches in India.

### Desalination Technologies and Processes

- Pages: 65
- Focus: Mechanisms, merits, and demerits of Thermal and Membrane desalination technologies, and recent innovations.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 3

#### Evidence Bullets

- IIT Bombay scientists developed Lotus leaf-like Solar Evaporators using a hydrophobic Graphene-based material for water desalination.
- Thermal desalination involves heating saline water and collecting condensed vapor, commonly used for seawater desalination.
- Low Temperature Thermal Desalination (LTTD) plants are operational in Lakshadweep islands like Kavaratti, Minicoy, and Agatti.
- Membrane technology uses semi-permeable membranes to filter dissolved solids, primarily for brackish water desalination.
- Membrane technologies are susceptible to fouling and may not fully remove Total Dissolved Solids (TDS) or pathogens.

#### Section Excerpt

Recently, IIT Bombay Scientists develop Lotus leaf-like Solar Evaporators for Salt-water Treatment, through a new hydrophobic Graphene-based material... Thermal Technology Concept: Heating of saline water and collecting condensed vapor... Example: Low Temperature Thermal Desalination (LTTD) plants in Kavaratti, Minicoy and Agatti Islands in Lakshadweep. Membrane Technology Concept: Feedwater is pumped through semi-permeable membranes to filter out dissolved solids. Usage: Brackish water desalination... Example: Nemmeli Seawater Desalination Plant, Tamil Nadu.

#### Claims

- IIT Bombay scientists developed Lotus leaf-like Solar Evaporators using a hydrophobic Graphene-based material for water desalination.
- Low Temperature Thermal Desalination (LTTD) plants, which heat saline water and collect condensed vapor, are operational in Lakshadweep islands such as Kavaratti, Minicoy, and Agatti.
- Membrane desalination technology utilizes semi-permeable membranes to filter dissolved solids but is susceptible to fouling and may not completely remove Total Dissolved Solids (TDS) or pathogens.

## Industry Classification and Hazardous Waste Rules

- Topic: Waste Management
- Pages: 66-67
- Rationale: Explains the PI-based color coding of industries and the 2025 Hazardous Waste Amendment Rules regarding EPR.

### Environment Protection (Management of Contaminated Sites) Rules, 2025

- Pages: 66-67
- Focus: Key provisions, exclusions, and liabilities under the new Contaminated Sites Rules.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Notified by MoEFCC to manage sites polluted by hazardous waste disposal.
- Explicitly excludes contamination from radioactive waste, mining, oil spills at sea, and solid waste dumps.
- SPCBs identify the polluter; if the contaminated land is sold, the new owner assumes liability.
- The polluter must execute and fund a clean-up plan through an approved agency.
- Initial assessment costs may be covered by the Environmental Relief Fund under the Public Liability Insurance Act, 1991.

#### Section Excerpt

MoEFCC notified Environment Protection (Management of Contaminated Sites) Rules, 2025. Contaminants Covered: Hazardous substances under Hazardous and Other Wastes Rules, 2016. Exclusions: Contamination from radioactive waste, mining, oil spills at sea, and solid waste dumps... Polluter Identification: SPCBs identify the polluter. If land is sold, the new owner is liable. Clean-Up Plan: Polluter must carry out a clean-up plan... Initial assessment costs may be covered by Central Government from Environmental Relief Fund under Public Liability Insurance Act, 1991.

#### Claims

- The Environment Protection (Management of Contaminated Sites) Rules, 2025 explicitly exclude contamination from radioactive waste, mining, oil spills at sea, and solid waste dumps.
- Under the Management of Contaminated Sites Rules, 2025, if contaminated land is sold, the new owner assumes liability for the pollution.
- State Pollution Control Boards (SPCBs) are the designated authorities responsible for identifying the polluter under the Management of Contaminated Sites Rules, 2025.
- Initial assessment costs for contaminated sites may be covered by the Central Government from the Environmental Relief Fund established under the Public Liability Insurance Act, 1991.

### Classification of Industries based on Pollution Index (PI)

- Pages: 66
- Focus: Criteria, categories, and background of industrial classification by CPCB/MoEFCC.
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Pollution Index (PI) ranges from 0 to 100, formulated in 2016 based on the Precautionary Principle evaluating water, air, and hazardous waste.
- Red Category (PI > 80) industries are normally not permitted in ecologically fragile or protected areas.
- White Category (PI < 25) industries are non-polluting and do not require Environmental Clearance (EC) or Consent.
- Blue Category includes Essential Environmental Services (ESSs) like Municipal Solid Waste Management and sewage treatment plants.
- The classification of industries originated in 1989 with the Doon Valley Notification issued by MoEFCC.

#### Section Excerpt

Revised methodology followed based on Pollution Index (PI) - ranges from 0 to 100 with increasing value indicating increased degree of pollution load. PI is determined based on Precautionary Principle... formulated in 2016... Categories: Red (PI > 80): No Red category industries shall normally be permitted in ecologically fragile area. Orange (55 <= PI < 80). Green (25 <= PI < 55). White (PI < 25): Non-polluting; do not require Environmental Clearance (EC) and Consent. Blue: Includes Essential Environmental Services (ESSs) for management of waste... Originated in 1989 with Doon Valley (Uttarakhand) Notification.

#### Claims

- The Pollution Index (PI) for classifying industries ranges from 0 to 100 and is formulated based on the Precautionary Principle.
- Red Category industries have a Pollution Index greater than 80 and are normally not permitted in ecologically fragile or protected areas.
- White Category industries have a Pollution Index below 25, are considered non-polluting, and do not require Environmental Clearance (EC) or Consent.
- The Blue Category of industries includes Essential Environmental Services (ESSs) such as Municipal Solid Waste Management and sewage treatment plants.
- The classification of industries based on pollution originated in 1989 with the Doon Valley Notification issued by the MoEFCC.

### Hazardous and Other Wastes (Management and Transboundary Movement) Amendment Rules, 2025

- Pages: 67
- Focus: Introduction of Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR) for non-ferrous metals and recycling targets.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Introduces an Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR) framework specifically for Scrap of Non-Ferrous Metals like aluminium, copper, and zinc.
- Recycling targets progressively increase from 10% in 2026-2027 to 75% by 2032-2033.
- The Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) is responsible for generating EPR certificates through a portal.
- EPR certificates are valid for two years from the end of the financial year in which they are generated.

#### Section Excerpt

Hazardous and Other Wastes (Management and Transboundary Movement) Amendment Rules, 2025 were notified by MoEFCC... Introduces comprehensive Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR) framework for Scrap of Non-Ferrous Metals (including aluminium, copper and zinc and its alloys)... Targets: Every producer shall be responsible for recycling target structure, which progressively increases from 10% in 2026-2027 to 75% by 2032-2033. Central Pollution Control Board: Shall generate an EPR certificate... Validity of the EPR certificate: Two years from the end of financial year when generated.

#### Claims

- The Hazardous and Other Wastes Amendment Rules, 2025 introduce an Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR) framework specifically for scrap of non-ferrous metals such as aluminium, copper, and zinc.
- Under the 2025 Amendment Rules, the recycling target for producers of non-ferrous metal scrap progressively increases from 10% in 2026-2027 to 75% by 2032-2033.
- The Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) is the designated authority responsible for generating Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR) certificates for non-ferrous metal scrap.
- Extended Producer Responsibility (EPR) certificates generated under the 2025 Amendment Rules are valid for two years from the end of the financial year in which they are generated.

## River Cities Alliance and Secondary Pollutants

### Ground-Level Ozone (GLO)

- Pages: 68-69
- Focus: Formation mechanisms, sources, and impacts of Ground-Level Ozone.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- It is a secondary, short-lived pollutant.
- Created by chemical reactions between oxides of nitrogen (NOx) and volatile organic compounds (VOCs).
- Natural sources include soil-based NOx emissions, wildfire-induced CO, and biospheric methane.
- Safe eight-hourly ozone standard is 100 micrograms/cubic metre.
- Worsens bronchitis, triggers asthma, and is a major component of smog.

#### Section Excerpt

Secondary, short-lived pollutant that remains in atmosphere for only hours to weeks. Created by chemical reactions between oxides of nitrogen (Nox) and volatile organic compounds (VOC).

#### Claims

- Ground-Level Ozone is a secondary, short-lived pollutant that remains in the atmosphere for hours to weeks.
- Ground-Level Ozone is created by chemical reactions between oxides of nitrogen (NOx) and volatile organic compounds (VOCs).
- Natural sources contributing to Ground-Level Ozone formation include soil-based NOx emissions, wildfire-induced CO, and biospheric methane.
- The safe eight-hourly standard for Ground-Level Ozone is 100 micrograms per cubic metre.
- Ground-Level Ozone is a major component of smog and exacerbates respiratory conditions like bronchitis and asthma.

- Prioritizes sites containing over 45,000 MT of legacy wastes, with no minimum threshold for UTs and North Eastern States.
- Key technologies include Biocapping (transforming dumpsites into green zones) and Biomining (using microorganisms to extract economic materials).

#### Section Excerpt

It was launched under the Swachh Bharat Mission-Urban (SBM-U) 2.0. It is a year-long, targeted initiative to achieve the goals of Lakshya Zero Dumpsites by September 2026. Objective: Prioritize high-impact locations, covering ~8.8 crore MT of legacy waste.

#### Claims

- The Dumpsite Remediation Accelerator Programme (DRAP) was launched under the Swachh Bharat Mission-Urban (SBM-U) 2.0 by the Ministry of Housing and Urban Affairs (MoHUA).
- DRAP is a targeted initiative aimed at achieving the goals of Lakshya Zero Dumpsites by September 2026.
- Under DRAP, priority is given to sites with over 45,000 MT of legacy wastes, but there is no minimum threshold for Union Territories and North Eastern States.
- Biocapping and Biomining are key technologies used in DRAP, where Biocapping transforms dumpsites into green zones and Biomining uses microorganisms to extract economic materials.

### River Cities Alliance (RCA)

- The Urban River Management Plan (URMP) Framework was launched in 2020 by the National Institute of Urban Affairs (NIUA) and the National Mission for Clean Ganga (NMCG).
- Chhatrapati Sambhaji Nagar's Kham River Restoration Mission was globally recognised by the WRI Ross Centre Prize for Cities.

### Per- and Polyfluoroalkyl Substances (PFAS)

- Pages: 69
- Focus: Properties, applications, and environmental persistence of PFAS.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Contain very strong carbon-fluorine bonds that do not degrade easily.
- Known as 'Forever Chemicals' due to their persistence in the environment.
- They resist grease, oil, water, and heat.
- Commonly used in cookware, food packaging, fabrics, and foams.
- Researchers recently discovered nearly 20 species of bacteria capable of breaking them down.

#### Section Excerpt

Chemicals having carbon-fluorine bonds, which are very strong and do not degrade easily. They can resist grease, oil, water, and heat. Impact: Persists in environment (known as ‘Forever Chemicals’).

#### Claims

- Per- and Polyfluoroalkyl Substances (PFAS) contain very strong carbon-fluorine bonds that make them highly resistant to degradation.
- PFAS are commonly referred to as 'Forever Chemicals' because of their extreme persistence in the environment.
- The ability of PFAS to resist grease, oil, water, and heat makes them suitable for use in everyday products like cookware, food packaging, fabrics, and foams.
- Certain species of bacteria have been discovered that are capable of breaking down Per- and Polyfluoroalkyl Substances (PFAS).

### Secondary Pollutants

- Pages: 69
- Focus: Definition, formation, and examples of secondary atmospheric pollutants.
- Question quota hint: 1
- Claim count: 3

#### Evidence Bullets

- Account for nearly one-third of India's PM2.5 pollution according to a CREA study.
- Formed by interactions between primary pollutants, sunlight, and atmospheric conditions.
- Examples include Ozone (O3), sulfuric acid (H2SO4), and nitric acid (HNO3).

#### Section Excerpt

Formed in atmosphere due to chemical/physical interactions between primary pollutants themselves or between primary pollutants and other atmospheric components. Examples: Ozone (O3), sulfuric acid (H2SO4), nitric acid (HNO3).

#### Claims

- Secondary pollutants are formed in the atmosphere through chemical or physical interactions between primary pollutants themselves, or between primary pollutants and other atmospheric components such as sunlight.
- Ozone (O3), sulfuric acid (H2SO4), and nitric acid (HNO3) are classified as secondary pollutants.
- Secondary pollutants account for nearly one-third of India's PM2.5 pollution.

### Toxic Heavy Metals

- Pages: 69
- Focus: Characteristics, sources, and specific health impacts of heavy metal pollution.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Characterized by high atomic weight and density greater than 4000 kg/m3.
- They are nondegradable and bioaccumulate in soils over decadal timescales.
- Cadmium exposure can cause kidney and bone damage, and lung cancer.
- Lead exposure causes developmental and neurobehavioral effects on fetuses.
- Arsenic is highly toxic in its inorganic form and can cause skin lesions, cardiovascular disease, and diabetes.

#### Section Excerpt

Heavy metals/metalloids are nondegradable and accumulate over decadal timescales in soils. Characteristics: High atomic weight ranging from 63.5 to 200.6 and density >4000 kg/m3.

#### Claims

- Heavy metals are characterized by a high atomic weight ranging from 63.5 to 200.6 and a density greater than 4000 kg/m3.
- Heavy metals and metalloids are nondegradable and bioaccumulate in soils over decadal timescales.
- Exposure to Cadmium can cause kidney damage, bone damage, and lung cancer.
- Lead exposure causes developmental and neurobehavioral effects on fetuses.
- Inorganic arsenic is highly toxic and can cause skin lesions, cardiovascular disease, and diabetes.

Union Jal Shakti Minister led a meeting focused on (E-flow) of Ganga River and its tributaries. About E-Flow: Refers to quantity, timing, and quality of water flow required to sustain freshwater ecosystems and livelihoods that depend on them. Benefits: Maintains ecological integrity of rivers and their estuaries. Extensive interventions in river flow like construction of dams, pollution, and encroachments have severely impacted ecological balance of rivers.

### Flue Gas Desulphurisation (FGD)

- Pages: 70
- Focus: Mechanism and policy regarding FGD in coal-fired power plants.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 3

#### Evidence Bullets

- FGD involves the removal of Sulphur Dioxide (SO2) from the exhaust gases of coal-fired power plants.
- It uses a scrubbing technique that typically involves a sodium- or calcium-based alkaline reagent.
- A recent study recommended limiting the FGD mandate to plants using imported or high-sulphur (>0.5%) coal rather than all plants.

#### Section Excerpt

A study commissioned by Office of the Principal Scientific Adviser recommended rollback of 2015 policy mandating installation of FGD systems in all of India's coal-fired plants. Rather, it recommended FGD to only those plants using imported coal or high (>0.5%) sulphur coal. About FGD: Involves removal of SO2 (Sulphur Dioxide) in exhaust gases from coal-fired power plants before being released to atmosphere. Uses scrubbing technique involving alkaline reagent (typically a sodium- or calcium-based alkaline reagent). Types of FGD Systems: Dry Sorbent Injection, Wet Limestone Based, Sea Water Based, etc.

#### Claims

- Flue Gas Desulphurisation (FGD) is a process used to remove Sulphur Dioxide (SO2) from the exhaust gases of coal-fired power plants.
- The FGD process utilizes a scrubbing technique that typically involves sodium-based or calcium-based alkaline reagents.
- A recent study recommended limiting the mandatory installation of FGD systems to coal plants using imported coal or high-sulphur coal (greater than 0.5 percent sulphur) rather than all plants.

### Green Firecrackers

- Pages: 70
- Focus: Composition, certification, and types of green firecrackers (SWAS, SAFAL, STAR).
- Question quota hint: 3
- Claim count: 7

#### Evidence Bullets

- Green firecrackers do not contain barium, which is conventionally used to add green color.
- They use Potassium Nitrate as an oxidant and have low thermite and minimal aluminium.
- They reduce particulate matter emissions by at least 30%.
- They are identified and certified by the CSIR NEERI logo.
- SWAS (Safe Water & Air Releaser) emits fine water droplets to absorb dust.
- STAR (Safe Thermite Cracker) is free from potassium nitrate and sulfur.

#### Section Excerpt

Easing temporarily the blanket ban on firecrackers in Delhi-NCR, Supreme Court allowed use of government-approved green crackers devoid of harmful chemicals and reducing air pollution. About Green Firecrackers: Composition: Low Thermite amount, minimum usage of Aluminium. Does not contain barium substance, used in firecrackers to add green colour. Uses Potassium Nitrate as oxidant. Benefit: Reduce at least 30 % emissions using particulate matter. Identification and Certification: CSIR NEERI Logo. Types of Green Firecrackers: SWAS (Safe Water & Air Releaser): Emit very fine water droplets that absorb dust. SAFAL (Safe Minimal Aluminium): Contain safe amount of aluminium & are quieter. STAR (Safe Thermite Cracker): Free from potassium nitrate and sulfur; emit minimal smoke.

#### Claims

- Green firecrackers do not contain barium, a substance conventionally used in firecrackers to produce a green color.
- Green firecrackers use Potassium Nitrate as an oxidant and contain low amounts of thermite and minimal aluminium.
- Green firecrackers reduce particulate matter emissions by at least 30% compared to conventional firecrackers.
- Green firecrackers are identified and certified by the CSIR NEERI logo.
- SWAS (Safe Water & Air Releaser) green firecrackers emit fine water droplets that absorb dust.
- STAR (Safe Thermite Cracker) green firecrackers are free from potassium nitrate and sulfur, and emit minimal smoke.
- SAFAL (Safe Minimal Aluminium) green firecrackers contain a safe amount of aluminium and are quieter than conventional crackers.

### Taj Trapezium Zone (TTZ)

- Pages: 70
- Focus: Geographical coverage, statutory backing, and regulatory framework of the TTZ.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- The TTZ covers a 10,400 sq km trapezoid-shaped area to prevent pollution around the Taj Mahal.
- It encompasses three World Heritage Sites: Taj Mahal, Agra Fort, and Fatehpur Sikri.
- The TTZ Pollution Authority is established under the Environment Protection Act, 1986.
- Industries in the zone are categorized into Red, Green, Orange, and White by the CPCB based on their pollution potential.

#### Section Excerpt

Supreme Court directed NEERI to assess the impact of neighbouring glass industry on Taj Mahal. SC in past issued orders from time to time for TTZ. About TTZ: Location: 10,400 sq km area (shaped like trapezoid) around Taj Mahal for preventing its pollution. Coverage: Three World Heritage Sites- Taj Mahal, Agra Fort, and Fatehpur Sikri. Taj Trapezium Zone Pollution (Prevention and Control) Authority: Under Environment Protection Act, 1986. Categories of Industries: Red, Green, Orange, and White based on pollution potential, as defined by the Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB).

#### Claims

- The Taj Trapezium Zone (TTZ) encompasses three World Heritage Sites: the Taj Mahal, Agra Fort, and Fatehpur Sikri.
- The Taj Trapezium Zone Pollution (Prevention and Control) Authority is established under the Environment Protection Act, 1986.
- Industries within the Taj Trapezium Zone are categorized into Red, Green, Orange, and White by the Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) based on their pollution potential.
- The Supreme Court directed the National Environmental Engineering Research Institute (NEERI) to assess the impact of the neighbouring glass industry on the Taj Mahal.

### Air Quality and Pollution Reports

#### Evidence Bullets

- The State of Global Air Report (SoGA) 2025 is released by the Health Effects Institute (HEI) and IHME (USA).
- According to SoGA 2025, India has the third highest exposure to Ozone Pollution globally.
- The Air Quality Life Index (AQLI) is developed by the Energy Policy Institute at the University of Chicago (EPIC) and measures particulate pollution's impact on life expectancy.
- Meeting WHO air quality guidelines could extend Delhi residents' lives by 8.2 years according to the AQLI.
- The Global Trade Update on Mobilising trade to curb Plastic Pollution is released by UNCTAD.

#### Section Excerpt

State of Global Air Report (SoGA) 2025 Released by: Health Effects Institute (HEI) and Institute for Health Metrics and Evaluation (IHME), USA. Mortality: Around 2 million deaths in 2023 linked to air pollution contributing 52% global share. Ozone Pollution: India has third highest exposure. 2025 Annual Update of the Air Quality Life Index (AQLI) Developer: Michael Greenstone and others at Energy Policy Institute at University of Chicago (EPIC). Measures the impact of particulate air pollution on life expectancy. Key Highlights: Air pollution meeting WHO guidelines could extend Indian residents' lives by 3.5 years on average, with Delhi seeing an 8.2-year gain. South Asia continues to be World's most polluted region. Global Trade Update on Mobilising trade to curb Plastic Pollution Released by: UNCTAD.

#### Claims

- The State of Global Air Report (SoGA) 2025 is released by the Health Effects Institute (HEI) and the Institute for Health Metrics and Evaluation (IHME).
- According to the State of Global Air Report (SoGA) 2025, India has the third highest exposure to Ozone Pollution globally.
- The Air Quality Life Index (AQLI) is developed by the Energy Policy Institute at the University of Chicago (EPIC).
- The Air Quality Life Index (AQLI) specifically measures the impact of particulate air pollution on life expectancy.
- The Global Trade Update on Mobilising trade to curb Plastic Pollution is released by the United Nations Conference on Trade and Development (UNCTAD).

## SDG Progress and Financing for Development

#### Evidence Bullets

- SDGs were adopted in 2015 (17 goals, 169 targets), succeeding the Millennium Development Goals (MDGs).
- India ranked 99th (score 67/100) in the 10th Sustainable Development Report (SDR) 2025, entering the top 100 for the first time.
- The Sustainable Development Report (SDR) is released by the UN Sustainable Development Solutions Network (SDSN).
- The Sustainable Development Goals Report (SDGR) is the only official UN report monitoring global progress, prepared by UN DESA.
- NITI Aayog releases the SDG India Index, District SDG Index for NER, and India's Voluntary National Review (VNR) Report.
- MoSPI launched the National Indicator Framework (NIF) Progress Report 2025.

#### Section Excerpt

UN Sustainable Development Goals (SDG) completed 10 years of adoption since September 2015. In 2015, all United Nations countries adopted 2030 Agenda for Sustainable Development. Sets out 17 SDGs and 169 targets. For the first time, India ranks among top 100 in SDG Index at 99 in 2025 with a score of 67 out of 100. Sustainable Development Report (SDR) 2025 Released by: SDG Transformation Centre, under Sustainable Development Solutions Network (SDSN). Sustainable Development Goals Report (SDGR) 2025 Prepared by: UN DESA. SDG India Index: Released by: NITI Aayog. Voluntary National Review (VNR) Report 2025: Released by: NITI Aayog.

#### Claims

- The Sustainable Development Goals (SDGs) were adopted in 2015, comprising 17 goals and 169 targets, and succeeded the Millennium Development Goals (MDGs).
- India ranked 99th in the Sustainable Development Report (SDR) 2025, marking its first time entering the top 100 in the SDG Index.
- The Sustainable Development Report (SDR) is released by the UN Sustainable Development Solutions Network (SDSN).
- The Sustainable Development Goals Report (SDGR) is the only official UN report monitoring global progress and is prepared by UN DESA.
- NITI Aayog is responsible for releasing the SDG India Index, the District SDG Index for NER, and India's Voluntary National Review (VNR) Report.
- The National Indicator Framework (NIF) Progress Report 2025 was launched by the Ministry of Statistics and Programme Implementation (MoSPI).

### Financing for Sustainable Development and the Sevilla Commitment

- Pages: 73
- Focus: The outcomes of the 4th International Conference on Financing for Development (FFD4) and the new financing mechanisms introduced under the Sevilla Commitment.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- The Sevilla Commitment was adopted at the 4th International Conference on Financing for Development (FFD4) to close the $4 trillion annual SDG financing gap.
- The United States exited the Sevilla Commitment process entirely.
- Debt-for-Development Swap Programme cancels or reduces debt in exchange for government spending on development goals.
- SCALED is a blended finance platform combining public, philanthropic, and private capital for sustainable development.
- Coalition for Global Solidarity Levies (led by France, Kenya, Barbados) aims to tax premium-class flying and private jets for climate action.
- Debt Swaps for Development Hub is led by Spain and the World Bank to scale up debt swaps and lower debt service burdens.

#### Section Excerpt

Recently, the 4th International Conference on Financing for Development (FFD4) adopted the final outcome document Compromiso de Sevilla, also known as Sevilla Commitment, to address the SDG financing gap in developing countries. Adopted by consensus, lays out a path to close the $4 trillion annual SDG financing gap. United States decided to exit the process entirely. New financing mechanisms announced: Debt-for-Development Swap Programme, Debt Swaps for Development Hub (led by Spain and the World Bank), Blended finance platform SCALED, Coalition for Global Solidarity Levies (led by France, Kenya and Barbados) aims to tax premium-class flying and private jets.

#### Claims

- The Sevilla Commitment was adopted at the 4th International Conference on Financing for Development to address the $4 trillion annual SDG financing gap.
- The United States completely exited the Sevilla Commitment process, which was otherwise adopted by consensus.
- The Debt Swaps for Development Hub is a new financing mechanism led by Spain and the World Bank to scale up debt swaps.
- SCALED is a blended finance platform that combines public, philanthropic, and private capital for sustainable development.
- The Coalition for Global Solidarity Levies, led by France, Kenya, and Barbados, aims to tax premium-class flying and private jets to fund climate action.

### International Solar Alliance (ISA) and Recent Initiatives

- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Mauritius became the first African country to sign the ISA's Country Partnership Framework (CPF).
- SUNRISE is a global initiative launched at the 8th ISA Assembly focusing on circularity and end-of-life management of solar PV and batteries.
- Following a 2020 amendment, all UN members are eligible to join the ISA, which currently has 104 members that have signed and ratified the agreement.
- ISA's 'Towards 1000' strategy aims to mobilize USD 1,000 billion in investments, deliver energy access to 1,000 million people, install 1,000 GW of solar capacity, and mitigate 1,000 million tonnes of CO2 annually by 2030.
- Key ISA initiatives include the SolarX Startup Challenge, STAR-C Initiative, Global Solar Facility, and Green Hydrogen Innovation Centre.

#### Section Excerpt

Mauritius Became 1st African Country to sign ISA's CPF... Launch of SUNRISE (Solar Upcycling Network for Recycling, Innovation & Stakeholder Engagement) - a global initiative on circularity and end-of-life (EoL) management of solar PV and batteries... Following 2020 amendment to its Framework Agreement, all UN members are now eligible to join. Mission: Guided by its Towards 1000 strategy, which aims to Mobilize USD 1,000 billions of investments... Deliver energy access to 1,000 million people...

#### Claims

- Mauritius is the first African country to sign the International Solar Alliance's Country Partnership Framework.
- The SUNRISE initiative is a global effort focused on the circularity and end-of-life management of solar PV and batteries.
- Following a 2020 amendment to its Framework Agreement, all United Nations member states are eligible to join the International Solar Alliance.
- The ISA's 'Towards 1000' strategy aims to mobilize USD 1,000 billion in investments and install 1,000 GW of solar capacity by 2030.
- The SolarX Startup Challenge, STAR-C Initiative, Global Solar Facility, and Green Hydrogen Innovation Centre are key initiatives under the International Solar Alliance.

- The Addis Ababa Action Agenda (2015) introduced Integrated National Financing Frameworks (INFFs) and the Technology Bank for Least Developed Countries.

#### Section Excerpt

Monterrey Consensus (2002) - Marked beginning of Financing for Development process at United Nations. It focused on increased Official Development Assistance (ODA)... Doha Declaration (2008) - It reaffirmed Monterrey Consensus... laid the foundation for Green Climate Fund. Addis Ababa Action Agenda (2015) - It introduced new financing frameworks for sustainable development such as Integrated National Financing Frameworks (INFFs), Technology Bank for Least Developed Countries etc.

#### Claims

- The Monterrey Consensus of 2002 marked the beginning of the United Nations Financing for Development process, focusing on Official Development Assistance.
- The Doha Declaration of 2008 laid the foundation for the Green Climate Fund.
- The Addis Ababa Action Agenda of 2015 introduced Integrated National Financing Frameworks (INFFs) and the Technology Bank for Least Developed Countries.

- Solar holds the largest share (39.4%) of India's non-fossil fuel installed capacity, followed by Wind (10.6%) and Hydro (10%).
- India ranks 4th globally in Renewable Energy Installed Capacity and Wind Power, and 3rd in Solar Power capacity.
- The top 5 renewable energy states in India are Rajasthan, Gujarat, Tamil Nadu, Karnataka, and Maharashtra.

In 2025 India's renewable energy sector achieved two historic milestones - renewable energy met 51.5% of total electricity demand (July 2025) and share of installed electricity capacity of non-fossil fuel sources reached 51% (September 2025). Achieving 50% of cumulative electric power installed capacity from non-fossil fuel-based energy resources by 2030 (achieved five years ahead)... Installed electricity capacity of non-fossil fuel sources: 256.09 GW... Global Ranking: 4th in Renewable Energy Installed Capacity.

#### Claims

- India achieved its NDC target of 50% cumulative electric power installed capacity from non-fossil fuels five years ahead of the 2030 deadline.

- Rajasthan is the top renewable energy state in India, followed by Gujarat and Tamil Nadu.
- India's Panchamrita targets for 2030 include reaching 500 GW of non-fossil energy capacity and meeting 50% of energy requirements from renewable energy.

- The relaxed efficiency norms apply only to off-grid applications like solar lanterns and micro solar grids, and do not affect larger systems like rooftop solar or pumps.

New Standard: Both technologies at 18% efficiency. Scope of Change: Applies only to off-grid solar applications below 200W like Solar lanterns, Micro solar grids... Introduction of Distributed Renewable Energy (DRE) category: To support smaller manufacturers & Wider rural electrification.

- The Approved List of Models and Manufacturers (ALMM) is a list of solar PV modules and manufacturers approved by the Ministry of New and Renewable Energy (MNRE).
- The relaxed 18% efficiency norms for solar panels do not apply to larger systems such as rooftop solar or solar pumps.
- A Distributed Renewable Energy (DRE) category was introduced to support smaller manufacturers and promote wider rural electrification.

## Solar Technologies and Green Hydrogen Mission

- Topic: Renewable Energy
- Pages: 76-77
- Rationale: Details Agri-PV, CSP, and the Green Hydrogen Certification Scheme (GHCI) under the National Mission.

### National Green Hydrogen Mission (NGHM) and Certification Scheme

- Pages: 76-77
- Focus: Green Hydrogen Certification Scheme of India (GHCI), hydrogen color typology, and NGHM targets.
- Question quota hint: 5
- Claim count: 7

#### Evidence Bullets

- The Bureau of Energy Efficiency (BEE) is the Nodal Authority for the Green Hydrogen Certification Scheme of India (GHCI).
- Green Certification is conditional on meeting an average emission intensity requirement of 2 kg CO2eq/kg H2 or less.
- Turquoise Hydrogen is produced through Pyrolysis using Methane, while Pink Hydrogen uses Electrolysis powered by Nuclear Energy.
- NGHM targets the production of 5 MMT per annum of Green Hydrogen by 2030.
- Deendayal Port, V.O. Chidambaranar Port, and Paradip Port have been recognized as Green Hydrogen Hubs under NGHM.
- Green hydrogen production requires up to 9 litres of water per kg of hydrogen and demands high electricity.

#### Section Excerpt

The Centre launched the Green Hydrogen Certification Scheme of India (GHCI) under NGHM, with MNRE as the ministry and the Bureau of Energy Efficiency (BEE) as the nodal authority. 'Green' certification requires an emission intensity of 2 kg CO2eq/kg H2 or less. Green hydrogen is produced via electrolysis using renewable energy. The text details various hydrogen types including Turquoise (Pyrolysis of Methane) and Pink (Electrolysis via Nuclear). NGHM targets 5 MMT per annum by 2030 and includes the SIGHT programme and Green Hydrogen Hubs like Deendayal, V.O. Chidambaranar, and Paradip ports.

#### Claims

- The Bureau of Energy Efficiency (BEE) serves as the Nodal Authority for the Green Hydrogen Certification Scheme of India (GHCI).
- Green Certification under the GHCI requires an average emission intensity of 2 kg CO2eq/kg H2 or less.
- Turquoise Hydrogen is produced through the pyrolysis of methane.
- Pink Hydrogen is produced using electrolysis powered by nuclear energy.
- The National Green Hydrogen Mission (NGHM) aims to achieve a production target of 5 MMT per annum of Green Hydrogen by 2030.
- Deendayal Port, V.O. Chidambaranar Port, and Paradip Port are recognized as Green Hydrogen Hubs under the National Green Hydrogen Mission.
- The production of green hydrogen is water-intensive, requiring up to 9 litres of water per kilogram of hydrogen produced.

### Solar Power Technologies and Agriphotovoltaics (APVs)

- Pages: 76
- Focus: Mechanisms of solar power generation and the dual-use model of Agriphotovoltaics.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Photovoltaic cells produce direct current (DC) which is converted to alternating current (AC) by an inverter.
- Concentrated Solar Power (CSP) harnesses energy by focusing sunlight using mirrors, lenses, and tracking devices.
- Agriphotovoltaics (APVs) combine agricultural production and electricity production on the same land area.
- APV systems utilize shade-tolerant crops to protect against strong solar irradiation and winds.

#### Section Excerpt

Solar power utilizes photovoltaic cells to produce direct current (DC), which is converted to alternating current (AC) by an inverter. Other technologies include Solar Thermal Energy for heat and Concentrated Solar Power (CSP) using mirrors and lenses. Agriphotovoltaics (APVs) or Solar Farming combines agricultural production and electricity generation on the same land, utilizing solar panels, tracking systems, and shade-tolerant crops to maximize land-use efficiency.

#### Claims

- Photovoltaic cells produce direct current (DC), which must be converted to alternating current (AC) by an inverter for standard use.
- Concentrated Solar Power (CSP) technology generates energy by focusing sunlight using mirrors, lenses, and tracking devices rather than directly converting light to electricity via photovoltaic effect.
- Agriphotovoltaics (APVs), or Solar Farming, is a dual land-use system that simultaneously combines agricultural crop production and solar electricity generation on the same land area.
- Agriphotovoltaic (APV) systems maximize land-use efficiency by utilizing shade-tolerant crops, which are protected from strong solar irradiation and winds by the solar panels.

### National Bioenergy Programme (NBP) and Revised Guidelines

- Pages: 77
- Focus: Objectives, components, and revised financial assistance guidelines of the National Bioenergy Programme.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- The National Bioenergy Programme provides 20% more Central Financial Assistance (CFA) for special categories like the North East Region, hilly states, and SC/ST beneficiaries.
- The Waste to Energy Programme supports projects generating Biogas, BioCNG, Power, or Syngas through technologies like pyrolysis and plasma gasification.
- Biogas is composed of 95% methane (CH4) and CO2, with traces of N2, H2, H2S, and O2.
- Revised guidelines for Waste to Energy introduce a two-stage CFA release: 50% after State Pollution Control Board consent and the remainder after 80% capacity achievement.
- The Biomass Programme supports stubble burning mitigation by allowing pellet producers in NCR to choose between MNRE or CPCB schemes.

#### Section Excerpt

MNRE updated guidelines for the National Bioenergy Programme (NBP), launched in 2022 to utilize surplus biomass for power generation. It provides Central Financial Assistance (CFA), with 20% extra for special categories like the North East Region and SC/ST beneficiaries. The NBP has three components: Waste to Energy (using pyrolysis and plasma gasification), Biomass (briquettes/pellets), and Biogas (95% methane and CO2). Revised guidelines offer easier approvals, two-stage CFA disbursal, IoT monitoring, and stubble burning support.

#### Claims

- The National Bioenergy Programme (NBP) provides an additional 20% Central Financial Assistance (CFA) for special categories, including the North East Region, hilly states, and SC/ST beneficiaries.
- The Waste to Energy component of the National Bioenergy Programme supports energy generation through advanced technologies such as pyrolysis and plasma gasification.
- Biogas is primarily composed of 95% methane (CH4) and carbon dioxide (CO2), along with trace amounts of other gases like hydrogen sulfide (H2S).
- The revised guidelines for the Waste to Energy Programme mandate a two-stage release of Central Financial Assistance, with the first 50% released after obtaining State Pollution Control Board consent.
- Under the Biomass Programme, pellet producers in the National Capital Region (NCR) have the option to choose between schemes from the MNRE or the Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) for stubble burning mitigation.

## Ethanol Blending and Alternative Biofuels

- Topic: Biofuels
- Pages: 78-79
- Rationale: Focuses on the EBP Programme targets, biofuel generations, and emerging fuels like E-Methanol and Bioethanol.

### Ethanol Blended Petrol (EBP) Programme and Ethanol Properties

- Pages: 78-79
- Focus: India's ethanol blending targets, properties of ethanol, feedstocks allowed under the National Policy on Biofuels, and government initiatives promoting blending.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- Target of 20% ethanol blending in petrol was advanced to 2025-26 from 2030 under the National Policy on Biofuels.
- Ethanol is 27% less energy-dense than petrol but has a high-octane rating.
- India is the world's third-largest producer and consumer of ethanol, after the USA and Brazil.
- Distillers' dried grains (DDGS) are a by-product of grain ethanol reused as animal feed.
- PM JIVAN Yojana facilitates Second Generation (2G) ethanol projects.
- GST on ethanol meant for the EBP Programme was reduced from 18% to 5%.

#### Section Excerpt

The EBP Programme, launched in 2003, aims to mix ethanol with petrol for cleaner fuel. The National Policy on Biofuels advanced the 20% blending target (E20) to 2025-26. Ethanol is a renewable, high-octane fuel produced via fermentation of sugars or petrochemical processes, but is 27% less energy-dense than petrol. India is the world's third-largest producer and consumer of ethanol. Initiatives include PM JIVAN Yojana for 2G ethanol, Ethanol Interest Subvention Schemes, and GST reduction to 5%.

#### Claims

- The target for achieving 20% ethanol blending in petrol (E20) was advanced to 2025-26 under the National Policy on Biofuels.
- Ethanol is a high-octane fuel but is 27% less energy-dense than petrol.
- India is the world's third-largest producer and consumer of ethanol, ranking behind the USA and Brazil.
- Distillers' dried grains (DDGS) are a by-product of grain ethanol production that are reused as animal feed.
- The PM JIVAN Yojana is specifically designed to facilitate and support Second Generation (2G) ethanol projects.
- The GST rate on ethanol meant for the Ethanol Blended Petrol (EBP) Programme has been reduced to 5%.

### Generations of Biofuels: 1G to 4G

- Pages: 78
- Focus: Classification of biofuels into first, second, third, and fourth generations based on their feedstocks.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- 1st Generation biofuels use edible feedstocks like wheat, corn, sugarcane, and palm oil.
- 2nd Generation biofuels use non-edible lignocellulosic biomass and waste, including Jatropha curcas and used cooking oil.
- 3rd Generation biofuels are produced from microalgae and cyanobacteria biomass.
- 4th Generation biofuels utilize genetically engineered microorganisms such as Escherichia coli.

#### Section Excerpt

Biofuels are categorized into four generations based on their feedstock. 1st Generation uses edible feedstocks like wheat, corn, sugarcane, and food-grade oils. 2nd Generation utilizes lignocellulosic biomass and waste, such as wheat bran, animal fats, used cooking oil, and Jatropha. 3rd Generation is derived from microalgae and cyanobacteria biomass. 4th Generation involves genetically engineered microorganisms like Escherichia coli and Saccharomyces cerevisiae.

#### Claims

- 1st Generation biofuels are produced using edible feedstocks such as wheat, corn, sugarcane, and food-grade oils.
- 2nd Generation biofuels utilize non-edible lignocellulosic biomass and waste materials, including Jatropha, animal fats, and used cooking oil.
- 3rd Generation biofuels are derived from microalgae and cyanobacteria biomass.
- 4th Generation biofuels involve the use of genetically engineered microorganisms such as Escherichia coli and Saccharomyces cerevisiae.

### Alternative Biofuels: Bioethanol, E-Methanol, and Compressed Biogas (CBG)

- Pages: 79
- Focus: Recent developments, production processes, and applications of Bioethanol (bamboo-based), E-Methanol, and CBG.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- Bamboo is an attractive 2G ethanol feedstock due to its high lignocellulose content and non-food nature.
- E-Methanol is a low-carbon fuel produced by combining green hydrogen with captured carbon dioxide.
- NITI Aayog spearheads the 'Methanol Economy' Programme in India, promoting blending of DME (Di-methyl Ether) in LPG.
- Raw biogas is produced by anaerobic digestion of biomass and waste sources.
- Biogas primarily consists of Methane (55-60%) and Carbon dioxide (35-40%), along with impurities like H2S.

#### Section Excerpt

India's first Bamboo-based Bio-Ethanol Plant was inaugurated in Assam; bamboo is a 2G ethanol feedstock due to high lignocellulose. The world's first commercial E-Methanol plant opened in Denmark. E-Methanol is produced by combining green hydrogen (from water electrolysis) with captured CO2. NITI Aayog's 'Methanol Economy' Programme promotes its use. India's First Cooperative Multi-Feed CBG Plant opened in Maharashtra. CBG is prepared from raw biogas (anaerobic digestion of biomass), which mainly contains 55-60% Methane and 35-40% Carbon dioxide.

#### Claims

- Bamboo is classified as a second-generation (2G) ethanol feedstock due to its high lignocellulose content and non-food nature.
- E-Methanol is a low-carbon fuel produced by combining green hydrogen, derived from water electrolysis, with captured carbon dioxide.
- The 'Methanol Economy' Programme in India, which promotes the blending of Di-methyl Ether (DME) in LPG, is spearheaded by NITI Aayog.
- Raw biogas is produced through the anaerobic digestion of biomass and primarily consists of 55-60% Methane and 35-40% Carbon dioxide.

## Geothermal Energy and Coal Gasification

- Topic: Alternative Energy
- Pages: 80-81
- Rationale: Covers the 2025 National Policy on Geothermal Energy and the financial incentives for coal gasification.

### Geothermal Energy and National Policy (NPGE, 2025)

- Pages: 80-81
- Focus: Geothermal energy basics, India's potential and provinces, and the National Policy on Geothermal Energy 2025.
- Question quota hint: 5
- Claim count: 6

#### Evidence Bullets

- High-enthalpy geothermal resources (~200°C) are suitable for electricity generation, while low-to-medium (100-180°C) are for direct use.
- India's estimated geothermal potential is 10,600 MW, with Puga and Chumathang in Ladakh being the most promising sites.
- India has 10 Geothermal Provinces; Himalayan, Naga-Lushai, and Andaman Nicobar are orogenic, while the rest are non-orogenic.
- NPGE 2025 allows extracting geothermal energy from abandoned oil/gas wells and recovering mineral by-products like lithium and cesium under the MMDR Act.
- Exploration leases for geothermal can be granted for 3-5 years, and development leases for up to 30 years.
- Northeast's first geothermal well is located at Dirang, Arunachal Pradesh, developed with Norway and Iceland.

#### Section Excerpt

Geothermal energy harnesses Earth's crust heat. High-enthalpy (~200°C) resources suit electricity generation; low-medium (100-180°C) suit direct use. India's potential is 10,600 MW. GSI identified 381 hot springs and 10 provinces (Himalayan, Naga-Lushai, Andaman are orogenic). Puga and Chumathang in Ladakh are promising. NPGE 2025 (MNRE) aims to make geothermal a major renewable pillar for net-zero by 2070. It covers EGS, AGS, abandoned oil wells, and mineral by-products (silica, lithium). Financing includes RE-RTD (up to 100% for govt, 70% for industry). Recent steps include SCCL's 20 kW pilot in Telangana and Northeast's 1st well at Dirang, Arunachal Pradesh.

#### Claims

- High-enthalpy geothermal resources (around 200 degrees Celsius) are suitable for electricity generation, whereas low-to-medium enthalpy resources (100-180 degrees Celsius) are suited for direct use.
- India's estimated geothermal potential is 10,600 MW, with Puga and Chumathang in Ladakh identified as the most promising sites.
- Among India's 10 Geothermal Provinces, the Himalayan, Naga-Lushai, and Andaman Nicobar provinces are classified as orogenic.
- The National Policy on Geothermal Energy (NPGE) 2025 permits the extraction of geothermal energy from abandoned oil and gas wells and the recovery of mineral by-products like lithium and cesium.
- Under the geothermal policy framework, exploration leases can be granted for 3 to 5 years, while development leases can extend up to 30 years.
- Northeast India's first geothermal well is located at Dirang in Arunachal Pradesh and was developed in collaboration with Norway and Iceland.

### Compressed Biogas (CBG) Initiatives

- Pages: 80
- Focus: Purification process of raw biogas and key Indian schemes promoting CBG.
- Question quota hint: 2
- Claim count: 4

#### Evidence Bullets

- CBG production requires purifying raw biogas to 90% methane and compressing it to 200-250 bar pressure.
- The GOBAR-DHAN scheme focuses on converting cattle dung and farm solid waste into Bio-CNG (CBG) and compost.
- The SATAT initiative aims to set up Compressed Biogas (CBG) plants for affordable transportation.

#### Section Excerpt

Raw biogas is purified to remove H2S, CO2, and water vapor, increasing methane concentration to 90%, and is then compressed to about 200-250 bar pressure to get CBG. Initiatives taken in India to promote CBG include the GOBAR-DHAN scheme to convert cattle dung and solid waste to Bio-CNG and compost, and the SATAT initiative for setting up CBG plants.

#### Claims

- Compressed Biogas (CBG) is produced by purifying raw biogas to remove hydrogen sulfide, carbon dioxide, and water vapor, achieving a methane concentration of 90%.
- To produce Compressed Biogas (CBG), purified biogas is compressed to a pressure of 200-250 bar.
- The GOBAR-DHAN scheme aims to convert cattle dung and farm solid waste into Bio-CNG (CBG) and compost.
- The SATAT initiative is designed to establish Compressed Biogas (CBG) plants to provide an affordable transportation fuel.

### Wind Energy and ALMM (Wind)

It mandates sourcing key components from approved Indian suppliers and data localization. India ranks 4th globally in installed wind power capacity, contributing 4.69% to total electricity. Wind potential is 1164 GW at 150 meters above ground level.

#### Claims

- The ALMM (Wind) framework mandates the sourcing of key wind turbine components from approved Indian suppliers and enforces data localization requirements.
- India ranks 4th globally in terms of installed wind power capacity.
- The National Institute of Wind Energy estimates India's wind power potential to be 1164 GW at 150 meters above ground level.

### Natural Farming Practices and Comparison with Organic Farming

- Pages: 84
- Focus: Core components of natural farming (Beejamrit, Jivamrit, Whapasa) and its distinctions from organic farming.
- Question quota hint: 4
- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Beejamrit involves the treatment of seeds using cow dung, urine, and lime-based formulations.
- Whapasa refers to activating earthworms in the soil for water vapour condensation.
- Organic Farming involves off-farm purchased inputs, whereas Natural Farming relies strictly on on-farm inputs based on the Desi Cow.
- Natural Farming does not allow the use of compost, vermicompost, or mined minerals for soil correction, unlike Organic Farming.

#### Section Excerpt

Natural Farming is a chemical-free, low-input system relying on on-farm inputs like Desi Cow products. Components include Beejamrit (seed treatment), Jivamrit (bio-stimulant), Mulching, and Whapasa (activating earthworms). Unlike Organic Farming, Natural Farming uses no external inputs, avoids compost/minerals for soil correction, and relies on surface decomposition rather than plowing.

#### Claims

- Beejamrit is a seed treatment component of Natural Farming that utilizes cow dung, urine, and lime-based formulations.
- Whapasa is a Natural Farming practice that refers to activating earthworms in the soil to facilitate water vapour condensation.
- Unlike Organic Farming, which allows off-farm purchased inputs, Natural Farming relies strictly on on-farm inputs based on the Desi Cow.
- Natural Farming does not permit the use of compost, vermicompost, or mined minerals for soil correction, whereas Organic Farming does.
- Natural Farming relies on surface decomposition rather than plowing.

- Claim count: 5

#### Evidence Bullets

- Model Rules 2025 require Felling Permits for agricultural lands with more than 10 trees, and NOCs for 10 or fewer trees.
- Applicants must register in the National Timber Management System with land ownership details.
- Agroforestry occupies approximately 8% of India's geographical land area.
- India joined the Bonn Challenge to restore 13 million hectares of degraded and deforested land by 2020.
- Agroforestry systems include Agrisilviculture (combines crops with tree crops) and Silvi-pasture (integrates livestock, forage, and trees).

#### Section Excerpt

The Government issued Model Rules 2025 for felling trees on agricultural land, overseen by State Level Committees. Applicants register in the National Timber Management System. Agroforestry occupies approximately 8% of India's land. Systems include Agrisilviculture (crops and trees), Agri-horticulture, and Silvi-pasture. Initiatives include the National Agroforestry Policy 2014, SubMission on Agroforestry, and India's Bonn Challenge pledge to restore 13 million hectares of degraded land by 2020.

#### Claims

- Under Model Rules 2025, felling trees on agricultural land requires a Felling Permit for more than 10 trees, and a No Objection Certificate (NOC) for 10 or fewer trees.
- The felling of trees on agricultural land under Model Rules 2025 is overseen by State Level Committees, requiring applicants to register in the National Timber Management System.
- Agroforestry occupies approximately 8 percent of India's geographical land area.
- In agroforestry systems, Agrisilviculture combines agricultural crops with tree crops, whereas Silvi-pasture integrates livestock, forage, and trees.
- India's pledge under the Bonn Challenge included restoring 13 million hectares of degraded and deforested land by the year 2020.
